STICKING TO THE FACTS
By Rev. William C. Kernon, Director
Institute for American Democracy
People who hold “pet” economic and financial
theories—and want to see reforms made to corres¬
pond with them—ought to be prepared to champion
them on their merits—and on their merits only.
This would seem to be the just and common
sense aproach. But with the Fascists and pro-Fas-
cists it never is. Intent upon establishing their posi¬
tion by arousing hatred—of Jews first, and of oth¬
ers later—they never fail to introduce their anti-
Semitic technique when discussing financial and
economic maters. This is particularly true when the
subject is international banking.
Now, international banking may or may not be
a good thing, a sound thing. No doubt, there is a
lot to be said on both sides of the question. Sincere
and reasonable men should be able to discuss it on
its merits. But not the Fascists. Opposed as they
are to international banking, they cannot tell you
why without blaming the Jews for everything in the
system which they do not like. For, say they, the
international bankers are Jews.
The facts tell another story. According to Poor’s
“Financial Records,” Fiscal Manual for 1940, there
were in that year 362 directors and trustees of
banks belonging to the New York Clearing House
Association. Twelve of them were Jews. Not one
Jewish director or trustee was in the Bankers Trust
Company; the Bank of New York; Central Hanover
Bank and Trust Company; Chase National Bank of
the City of New York; Chemical Bank and Trust
Company; Continental Banking and Trust Company
of New York; Corn Exchange Bank and Trust Com¬
pany; Federal Reserve Bank of New York; First
National Bank of City of New York; Guaranty
Trust Company of New York; Irving Trust Com¬
pany ; and Title Guaranty and Trust Company.
True, there are some Jewish banking firms in
the investment field. But they do not have the pow¬
er that non-Jewish firms have. The records as of
March 1, 1935, for example, show that non-Jewish
firms made the largest percentage of outstanding
t-
foreign loans: J. P. Morgan, 19.87%; National City
Co., 11.71%; Chase, Harris, Ferver, 8.45%; Guar¬
anty Company, 6.88%; Banc-Amerika-Blair, 6.18%;
Lee, Higginson, Co., 4.23%. The highest Jewish
firm was Kuhn, Loeb & Co., with 2.88%. Not an
impressive record in banking so far as the Jews are
concerned. Even less impressive when you consider
that the so-called Jewish houses are not exclusive¬
ly Jewish.
The story is the same in Great Britain. Not a
Jew on the Board of Directors of the Bank of Eng¬
land—not a Jewish firm even represented on the
Board. Only three Jews out of 150 directors of the
“Big Five”— and not one Jew a director of the
other Clearing House Banks.
In Germany, in 1940, Jewish influence was
strong in only 10 of the 1,657 joint-stock banks. In
France, before the war, non-Jews headed the five
lagest commercial, and the two largest investment
banks.
Americans believe in justice. And Americans
who hear false charges about the Jews and the evils
of international banking ought not only to know
the facts. They ought to serve the ends of justice
by answering with the facts.

Jacksonville Jewish Center
EDUCATIONAL PROGRAM
One of the most promising phases of the entire
Center progra mwas the expansion of its educational
system into a complete extensive program covering
all age groups in a year-around program. Up to sev¬
eral years ago, the system consisted merely of a
.'Sunday School and Hebrew School. During the past
three years a daily nursery-kindergarten and a sum¬
mer day camp were added to round out the pro¬
gram. Much remains to be accomplished in intensi¬
fying the curriculum and in integrating the various
^departments nito a single educational process, but
ithe principal framework for a system of intensive
'Jewish education has been founded.
HEBREW SCHOOL
The foundation of the entire educational pro¬
gram is the daily Hebrew School. Perhaps the
greatest achievement of the past year’s activity in
this department was the increase of the enrollment
from 60 to a total of 85 children at the end of the
year.
The students range in age from six to thirteen
and were divided into six classes. Emphasis in the
three elementary groups was placed on the teaching
of the mechanics of reading Hebrew and on the
fundamentals of the Hebrew language. In addition
to this curriculum, the three advanced classes stud¬
ied the early history of the Jewish people in story
form. The two higher classes also studied selections
from the book of Genesis and Exodus, with empha¬
sis being placed upon the content of the Sedras as
a whole and upon mastery of the more familiar pas¬
sages.
“KQLAYNU”
The children of the Hebrew School issued a
monthly paper, “Kolaynu,” in which appeared origi¬
nal stories, poems, quizes and editorials. “Kolaynu”
was edited by Joel Bressler, Gary Bloom, Helene
Zimmerman and Pearl Aronowitz.
JUNIOR CONGREGATION
The most important phase of the entire educa¬
tional program is the translation of the theoretical
program of the school into the practical experience
of everyday life. This is best accomplished through
the Junior Congregation. Beginning the year with
a congregation of five children, the attendance was
developed to an average of 35 regular congregants
on the Sabbath, with the total attendance on some
occasions exceeding 50. Services v/ere conducted by
the children themselves and were supplemented by
original sermonettes and stories on the portion of
the week. Kiddush was provided by parents of chil¬
dren who celebrated their birthdays and by the
Daughters of Israel.
In order to stimulate attendance at service and
Hebrew School a chart was kept of the attendance
record of every pupil, with over 30 children enjoying
theater parties for attaining a perfect attendance
record for at least ten weeks. Service ribbons were
awarded those learning specified brochos (bless¬
ings) and a Mogen Dovid pin to those who memor¬
ized passages from various prayers.
Special celebrations were held for each holiday
in the various classes and in assemblies. Of special
note were the closing exercises, at which each class
demonstrated the progress made during the year.
Prizes were awarded to the following for outstand¬
ing work during the course of the year: Pearl Aron¬
owitz, Lorraine Goldberg, Charles Haimowitz, Stan¬
ley Kantor, Franklin Selber, and Helene Zimmer¬
man.
The staff of the Hebrew School included Mrs.
Carl Alpert, principal; Mrs. Harry Abrams and Miss
Gertrude Meyerhoff, and Rev. J. Robin supervised
the preparation of the Bar Mitzvahs, as he has done
for tne past 14 years.
SUNDAY SCHOOL
The Sunday School consisting of ten regular
grades and two kindergarten groups completed a
year of continued progress. This one-day a week
school offers a ten-year curriculum in history, cus¬
toms and ceremonies. Twenty-seven children consti¬
tuting the largest graduating class in the history of
the institution were confirmed at impressive cere¬
monies held on May 20th, with Rabbi Solomon E.
Goldfarb delivering the confirmation sermon.
A special feature of the year’s work was the
introduction of inter-class competition in regular
Sabbath service attendance, in Sunday morning at¬
tendance and in Keren Ami (Social Service) contri¬
butions. The sixth grade, under the leadership of
Miss Eunice Davis, was adjudged the outstanding
class in the overall competition, with the Confirma¬
tion Class second. Bernard Heyman was declared
winner of the Hackel Trophy for the outstanding
school and the extra-curricular achievement of the
school.
Prizes in the Sunday School were awarded on
June 3rd as follows:
Grads Om: Prizes—Kenneth Chepenik, Ro¬
chelle Robin; Certificates—Henry Sobel, Sharon Sa¬
fer; Attendance—Marilyn Fletcher, Elaine Levin.
Grade Two: Prizes—Byron Selber, Elka Miriam
Block; Certificates—Charles Zimmerman, Lois
Sachs; Attendance—Ruben Mizrahi.

Jacksonville Jewish Center

BUILDING FUND DRIVE
Morris Wolfson
The past year witnessed the occurrence of an
epochal event in the history of the Jacksonville Jew¬
ish Center, when a campaign was launched to ac¬
quire funds for an extensive building program. The
impetus for the progressive move came from an an¬
nouncement at the annual meeting of the Center, by
Mr. Ben Setzer of a contribution of $10,000.00 to
initiate the drive.
A committee consisting of Abe Newman, chair¬
man; Morris Wolfson, Ben Setzer, Joe Becker, Jo¬
seph Hackel, Harry Gendzier, and Philip Selber
was immediately appointed to study the proposal
and to make a report to the Center Board. As a
result'of the committee’s recommendation, the Board
of Directors in March, 1945, overwhelmingly ap¬
proved the recommendation of the committee to
“continue its program established in 1926 to create
a complete synagogue center catering to the religi¬
ous, educational, cultural, social and recreational
needs of the Jewish community.”
The committee in its report stated: “We com¬
mend the institution for never having lost sight of
this objective, and for its action in attempting to
Newman
Ben Setzer
bring this desired program into effect. As condi¬
tions improved, the property adjacent to the Center
was obtained and the mortgage indebtedness of
$35,000.00 was completely removed. The founda¬
tion for further expansion was thus achieved and
the contribution of Mr. Ben Setzer has now made
the chances for fulfillment of such a program not
only possible, but probable.”
Since the endorsement of the program in Febru¬
ary, much work has taken place to acquaint the
general public with the needs and requirements of
the Center as to building facilities for the future.
A brochure is now being prepared under the direc¬
tion of Harry Broida, Secretary of the Center, to be
published early in the fall when the actual cam¬
paign for building funds will begin. The brochure
sets forth the history of the undertaking and the
general plans for the expansion. No decision is to
be made as to the location of future building facili¬
ties until such time as the actual plans are crystal¬
lized, since the conditions existing at that time will
determine the building site. Several large contribu¬
tions have already been indicated as being available
upon the inauguration of the forthcoming drive.

President

Mrs. H. J. Slott
The Pushaloter Ladies' Aid Society was formed
over 30 years ago to a'd worthy causes in Pushalot,
Lithuania. All members of the society either were
born in Pushalot or are married to natives of that
city. Until the Nazi invasion severed contacts with
the Jewish communities abroad, money collected
from the local members was sent to Pushalot at reg¬
ular intervals, for such causes as Passover, rebuild¬
ing of the Synagogue, etc. Today the group aids
indigent Pushaloters in this country and also con¬
tributes to local charities.

Jacksonville Jewish Center
DAY CAMP
Perhaps the greatest progress made during the
entire year was achieved in the organization of the
Center Day Camp. Over 60 children were among
the campers, who were able to take part in a varied
program conducted in a traditional Jewish environ¬
ment.
The daily schedule included a prayer service,
Hebrew instruction, athletics, arts and crafts, swim¬
ming, dramatics, and other similar camp activities.
Each week the children visited a place of interest
in the environment of the City and also spent a day
at the Beach every Tuesday. Every Friday a genu¬
ine traditional Shabbas spirit prevailed with the chil¬
dren dressed in blue and white while they conducted
a special Shabbas service. A Sabbath procession
headed by the Sabbath Queen inaugurated the cere¬
mony, and plays, created by the children themselves,
were presented.
During the course of its eight weeks’ existence,
the Day Camp instilled in the children a love of Jew¬
ish cultural values through their participation in
Palestinian singing and dancing and in their activi¬
ties in the arts and crafts course, the objects of
which were centered around Jewish motifs. Special
attention was devoted to the study of Hebrew.
C. H. BRO WN COMPANY

Jacksonville Jewish Center
t
■
RELIGIOUS ACTIVITIES
The Center occupies an unusual position in of¬
fering- religious facilities for both the orthodox and
conservative elements of the community.
ORTHODOX SERVICES
For these orthodox adherents, the institution
maintains a separate congregation on the High Holy
Days, featuring strict adherence to the orthodox
type of service, including segregation of sexes. It
also maintains a year-around cycle of orthodox serv¬
ices, such as the early Friday evening service (Kab-
ala Shabbas) and all holiday services. It has also
aided the orthodox group in all of its endeavors to
promote a more intensive type of orthodox adher¬
ence.
■)
')
)
c'
j CONSERVATIVE SERVICES
q On the other hand, while catering to the needs
of orthodox Jewry, the Center has with the same
interest and vigor continued to develop a conserva¬
tive type of service for those desiring this trend in
worship. As a result, late Friday evening services
have been developed into the most successful phase
of the year-around religious activities of the Center.
During the past year, in the absence of a permanent
Rabbi, a number of guest speakers have occupied
the Center pulpit on Friday evenings, including the
following: Chaplain Earl Stone upon his return from
overseas duty, Chaplain Isaiah Rackovsky, Chaplain
Leo Stillpass, Chaplain Sidney Greenberg, Chaplain
Richard Singer, Rabbi David H. Panitz of Syracuse,
New York, Rabbi Maurice Solomon of Kansas City,
Rabbi Uri Miller of New Orleans, Rabbi Aaron Wise,
of Schenectady, New York, Rabbi Sanders A. To-
field of Houston, Texas, Rabbi Solomon D, Goldfarb
of Charleston, South Carolina, Rabbi Henry B. Wer-
nick of Suffolk, Virginia, Mortimer May, President
of the Southeastern Region of the Z. O. A., Carl
Alpert, former Editor of the New Palestine, Student
Rabbi Benjamin Sincoff of the Jewish Theological
Seminary, and others.
Organizations participating in these services in¬
cluded the Zionist District, Hadassah, Young Juda¬
ean, Clubs, Boy Scouts, Girl Scouts, Daughters of
Israel, Junior Hadassah and A. Z. A. Melvin Margol
assumed the Cantorial duties connected with the
services and his splendid work and fine spirit of
cooperation throughout the year is greatly appre¬
ciated. Others who assisted in the rendition of the
services included Herbert Talisman and Harry Ab¬
rams. Several guest cantors appeared during the
course of the year, including the well known Cantor
Samuel Vigoda. J¡
SPECIAL SERVICES
Impressive Memorial services in memory of the
late President Roosevelt were held on Sunday, April
15th, in the Center Synagogue, with Rabbi Abraham
I. Rosenberg of Savannah conducting the services
and delivering the eulogy. On Sunday, May ig^-u
Thanksgiving services commemorating the Allied
victory in Europe were conducted by Chaplain Rich-
’ . ' * ,
ard Singer of Camp Blanding, Florida, who deliv¬
ered the principal address. On both occasions, Rev.
B. Safer participated, delivering the traditional “El
Mole Rachamim” in memory of the late President;.
The same prayer for those who had fallen in the
service of their country during the war was rendered
on the occasion of .the Thanksgiving services.
• - m
CONFIRMATION SABBATH
An innovation in the nature of a Confirmation
Sabbath was added to the regular religious observ¬
ances of the year. On the Friday evening preceding
the Confirmation exercises, the entire service was
conducted by members of the class with four mem-
bers, Harry Yoffee, Bernice Margol, Harold Haimo-
witz and Idlene Portnoy delivering short sermons.
On Saturday morning the male members of the class
were called to the Torah, with one member of the
class, Bernard Heyman, delivering the class sermon.
All of the holidays were fittingly observed
throughout the year and the daily minyon was main¬
tained.

JACKSONVILLE JEWISH CENTER
Boy Scouts
Officers
Scoutmaster
Irvin Rothstein
Assistant Scoutmaster
Albert Bressler
Jr. Asst. Scoutmaster
Mordy Rosenkranz
Jr. Asst. Scoutmaster
Sol Proctor
Troop Committee
Ben Chepenik, Chmn. Harry Eff, Co-Chmn.
Israel Schemer Mesod Bono Philip Selber
Troop Fourteen, Boy Scouts of America, which
is sponsored by the Jacksonville Jewish Center, is
at the present time undergoing a complete reorgani¬
zation in contemplation of the fall season.
Mr. Ben Chepenik, who has served for the past
three years as Scoutmaster of the Troop, is assum¬
ing the overall supervision of the program as Chair¬
man of the Troop Committee. Harry Eff, former
neighborhood Boy Scout Commissioner in St. Aug¬
ustine, has been appointed Assistant Chairman. The
committee was fortunate in securing the services
of Mr. Irvin Rothstein as Scoutmaster and Mr.
Albert Bressler as Assistant Scoutmaster. Mordy
Rosenkranz and Sol Proctor, who have been active
for a number of years in the troop, will assist in
the rolls of Junior Assistant Scoutmasters.
Scoutmaster Rothstein announced that appoint¬
ment of Troop leaders and officers will not be made
until some time in September, after the new roster
has been completed.
During the past year the troop took part in the
usual hikes, camping trips and other projects cons¬
tituting the Scout program. Eleven of the boys went
to camp as a unit during the last week of July.
Much is expected of the new re-organization
and members of the Center are looking forward
to the re-establishment of the troop as one of the
outstanding units in the city.

JACKSONVILLE JEWISH CENTER
I. M. Lieberman
Charles Kroser
CEMETERY ACTIVITIES
An outstanding achievement of the Jewish Center
taking place during the past year was the purchase
of a new cemetery tract, adjoining the Evergreen
Cemetery Association’s land on its western bound¬
ary. The plot consists of approximately eleven acres
and represents one of the finest locations available
for such purposes. No plans have been made for its
improvement, but a special committee has been ap¬
pointed to study plans fo rits future development.
The membership of the Cemtery Committee was
changed during the past year as the result of an
election held by the Board of Directors in February.
The new Committee consists of Max Rubin, chair¬
man; David Moscovitz, Max Mirkis, Joseph Hackel,
Oscar Mageris, I. M. Lieberman, Charles Kroser,
Hyman Selber, and Morris Wolf son. Oscar Magezis
has been placed in charge of the actual supervision
of the cemetery plot, while Rev. J. Robin continued
to act as chairman of the Shamoshim committee, in
charge of performing auxiliary duties to carrying
out burials according to orthodox ritual.

These Are the Facts!
Studies by the Bureau of War Records indicate that upwards of
500,000 men of Jewish faith are serving in the armed forces of the
United States; or, in military terms, more than 33 divisions.
More than 8,200 Jewish men in uniform have received citations
for valor and merit, many of them posthumously. Among the 8,200
are 78 Jewish men who each have to their cred t ten or mere awards
for valor, holding an aggregate of 881 such decorations.
There are 266 rabbis serving as chaplains, representing one-fourth
of the total American rabbinate. Fully 50 percent of all rabbis in
America have volunteered for duty as chaplains. Three Jewish chap¬
lains have lost their lives serving overseas.
Thirty-four percent of the Jewish male refugee population of the
United States between the ages of 18-45 are bearing arms for their
adopted country.
At least one-third of the Jewish physicians of the nation are serv¬
ing with the armed forces.
House-to-house canvasses in a number of representative cities show
that the percentage of Jewish men in service is slightly higher than the
ratio of Jews to the general population.
Thousands of Jewish families have given 3 or more sons to the
service; 1,059 of these families have contributed a total of 3,594 mem¬
bers to the armed forces; 81 of these families have given 448.
The Distinguished Service Cross, the second highest army award
for heroism, has been earned by more than 40 men of Jewish faith.
Of the 500.000 Jewish men in uniform,, the Bureau of War Records
has a comparative Hgure of more than 30,000 Jewish casualties in all
branches of the armed services.

The President’s Message
Though the trials and hardships that accompany the
discharge of our official duties as officers of the Center
oftimes seemes to far outweigh the satisfaction, secured
from a duty well performed, it is with a sincere feeling
of gratification and pleasure, that we look back on the
accomplishments of the Jacksonville Jewish Center dur¬
ing this past year.
The concrete evidence of progress nevertheless is in
itself sufficient reward. The launching of a build¬
ing drive, with an initial contribution of $10,000.00
from one member, and promise of support from many,
many others—complete renovation of our present build¬
ing—purchase of a new cemetery plot to take care of
some future generations—increase of Hebrew school
enrollment to over 85 regular attenders—election of a
capable, energetic, experienced young leader in Israel,
Rabbi David H. Panitz, to the spiritual leadership of
the Center—selection of a very capable musician, Rev.
Harry Bettman as Cantor—inauguration of a Day Camp
which exceeded our fondest dreams and which had an
attendance of over 60 campers during its 8 weeks ex¬
istence—the maintenance of our fine, loyal staff in the
Hebrew school—the organization of a Center Men s
Club, long a vital need of the Institution—the conclu¬
sion of the year without a financial deficit—the financing
of the coming year’s budget assured through a gener¬
ous response from the members who have eagerly in¬
creased their dues schedule—preparations in process of
completion for the launching of an intensive drive for the
Building Fund immediately after the High Holy Days—
development of a Junior Congregation with an aver¬
age attendance of over 25—reorganization of a Center
Library and other fine work by the Daughters of Israel
,—and above all, a year of constant study and delibera¬
tion of the problems of the institution and its future
iieeds and course of development.
In the message of the President contained in last year’s
Year Book, it was stated that the greatest step forward
yet taken by the Jacksonville Jewish Center was its de¬
cision to expand its activities in order to become a true
Center in the literal sense of the word. That we have
fulfilled the first phase in the achievement of this goal
cannot be denied by any one familiar with the real
accomplishments of the Center during the year. The
spirit of harmony that has been developed between all
groups will enable us to achieve even more during the
coming year, especially so because a complete profes¬
sional staff has been secured. If the membership is willing
to work, greater achievements can be attained.
The Center needs new leadership to add to its present
loyal leadership, men who will be willing to sacrifice
and be capable of work so the Institution may prosper
and grow. It is hoped that some of this leadership will
fc>e derived from the newly formed Mens Club. Many of
PATRONIZE OUR ADVERTISERS
HARRY GENDZIER
President
you, who have not been active should enlist under the
Banner of the Mens Club that you may furnish much
needed assistance in the promotion of the entire Cen¬
ter Program.
The New Building Program, details of which are con¬
tained elsewhere in the Year Book, is a tremendous
undertaking. The membership of the Center is financial¬
ly able to provide the building facilities needed by the
community. However, the forthcoming drive can only
be a success if the entire membership is willing to work
towards its success.
In conclusion may I state, that it has been a pleasure
and privilege, as well as a high honor to have served the
community for a second term as president of the Center.
My task has been eased, and many accomplishments
made possible, only through the contribution of time
and effort by those who so willingly and successfully
worked with me for the welfare of the Institution. To
these loyal workers I extend my deepest thanks.
May the coming year bring us closer to the realiza¬
tion of our cherished hopes and aspirations for the wel¬
fare of our people. May God grant us permanent Peace
this coming year and bring home to us the sons and
daughters now serving with the Armed Forces. May
God grant to each of us a happy, healthy and prosperous
New Year.
HARRY GENDZIER, President.

Jacksonville Jewish Center
(Continued from page 55)
JUNIOR CONGREGATION
In addition to the regular Saturday morning
services, a special Junior Congregation is held
throughout the school year, for the children. These
junior services were greatly improved during the
past year, both as to the type of service and as to
attendance. Mrs. Carl Alpert was in charge of this
activity, assisted by Miss Gertrude Meyerhoff, Ber¬
nard Heyman and Nathan Krestul.
AIN YAACOB CLASS
Rev. B. Safer, who has conducted the Ain Yaacob
Class on Saturday afternoons for a number of years,
continued this activity throughout all of the past
year. This class consists of a group of old men who
gather each Saturday afternoon to study the Ha-
gadic portions of the Talmud contained in the book
known as Ain Yaacob. Rev. Safer also conducted
all of the religious services held during the year, and
performed the duties usually performed by the
Rabbi.
Rev. J. Robin, who has held the position of Sex¬
ton for the past fourteen years, continued to dis¬
charge his usual duties during the past year, which
included the preparation of all Bar Mitzvahs taking
place during the year.
The religious program of the Center should ex¬
perience a considerable improvement with the ad¬
vent of the New Year. The election of Rabbi David
H. Panitz to the spiritual leadership of the institu¬
tion and the selection of Rev. Harry Beckman for the
position of Cantor should provide the institution with
an excellent staff for the rendition of religious serv¬
ices for all groups. Rev. B. Safer and Rev. J. Robin
will continue in the same capacities in which they
have served during the past year.
Rev. B. Safer Rev. Joseph Robin

Junior Hadassah

The outstanding Zionist youth organization in America is Junior Hadassah. Its 250
chapters have made great strides in bringing the message of Zion to its young women. Real¬
izing the critical time in which they are living, members of Junior Hadassah have determ¬
ined to devote themselves to the defense of America, to helping the allied nations and to the
building of a Jewish homeland in Palestine. By conducting this joint Palestinian and Amer¬
ican program, Junior Hadassah contributes daily to the war effort.
I
Junior Hadassah is solely responsible for the children’s village of Meir Shfeejah, which
has received a number of refugee children through Youth Aliyah. Through the Jewish Na¬
tional Fund, it is increasing the purchase of land for the setlement of more refugees and to
raise more food for the allied nations. The Henrietta Izold School of Nursing is supported
jointly by the Junior and Senior Hadassah. Another important phase of the Jewish National
Fund and for Youth Aliyah.
The Jacksonville unit of the organization, under the leadership of its officers and the
guidance of Senior Hadassah, has participated in all of these projects. It has sponsored its
annual Yom Kippur Dance, offered a “Baby-Sitters Service,” participated in J. N. F. box
collections and its members have also acted as waitresses at several community functions.
The organization has sponsored a number of U. S. O. Jewish Welfare Board affairs; it has
cooperated with community council. Leading Young Judaean clubs and participating in Fri¬
day night services are among its activities of the year.
The social events of the year included a membership drive highlighted by a buffet supper
and cocktail party, a brilliant Donar affair and also served several informal wiener roasts.
The year’s activities were culminated by an “Installation Party.”
The educational program of the organization fosters an understanding and appreciation
of Jewish life, its rich cultural contributions to civilization and the contemporary ideals that
motivate Zionism. Through the study of vital current events, history, and lives of great
figures in Judaism and the celebrations and observance of holidays, a pride in things Jewish
is founded. Moving pictures, dramatic skits, music, discussion groups, and lectures, are med¬
iums through which education is disseminated. The educational program is the foundation
upon which the organization is built; it is the basis of plans for the coming years. It is with
earnest anticipation that the unit looks to the future for continued success and progress.

Jacksonville Jewish Community Council
1945 Beneficiaries (Continued)
Overseas Causes
AMERICAN FRIENDS OF HEBREW UNIVERSITY—Supports the Hebrew University in Jeru¬
salem, a permanent home for the traditions of science and learning among Jews.
AMERICAN FUND FOR PALESTINIAN INSTITUTIONS—Supports 52 cultural, educational
and social service institutions which do not receive funds from any other American source.
AMERICAN ORT FEDERATION—Aids vocational retraining of Jews through educational and
technical schools, agricultural colonies, operatives, etc.
EZRAS TORAH—Grants assistance to orthodox rabbis and religious scholars in need.
EUROPEAN AND PALESTINIAN INSTITUTIONS—24 traditional charitable and educational
institutions receive allocations from an annual miscellaneous fund.
HEBREW SHELTERING AND IMMIGRANT AID SOCIETY — Conducts immigration activi¬
ties on a world-wide scale in collaboration with numerous local committees.
JEWISH TELEGRAPHIC AGENCY—Collects and distributes news about Jews all over the
world.
NATIONAL LABOR COMMITTEE FOR PALESTINE—Provides funds for cooperative indus¬
tries and labor settlements in Palestine.
UNITED JEWISH APPEAL—Comprises the following three agencies:
(1) JOINT DISTRIBUTION COMMITTEE—Major American Jewish agency for world¬
wide relief.
(2) NATIONAL REFUGEE SERVICE—Assists newcomers to this country.
(3) UNITED PALESTINE APPEAL—Central instrument for building the Jewish Na¬
tional Home in Palestine through the Jewish National Fund and Palestine Founda¬
tion Fund.
HADASSAH YOUTH ALIYAH—Brings children to Palestine from countries of Jewish perse¬
cution, and places them in agricultural and vocational schools.
AMERICAN JEWISH CONGRESS—Fights against anti-Semitism and economic discrimina¬
tion; endeavors to safeguard the rights of Jews everywhere.
NATIONAL COUNCIL OF JEWISH WOMEN—Promotes a program of war service, service to
foreign born, social legislation and civic cooperation.
National Causes
BNAI BRITH WIDER SCOPE—Maintains Hillel Foundations on college and university cam¬
puses, Vocational Service Bureau and AZA youth activities.
COUNCIL OF JEWISH FEDERATIONS AND WELFARE FUNDS — Coordinates activities of
welfare funds and offers services in research and planning.
HEBREW THEOLOGICAL COLLEGE OF CHICAGO—Trains religious leaders for orthodox
synagogues and instructors for religious schools.
HISTADRUTH IVRITH—Promotes knowledge of Hebrew language and literature in the Unit¬
ed States.
JEWISH LABOR COMMITTEE—Through labor and fraternal organizations fights to protect
religious, political and civil rights of Jews.
JEWISH CONSUMPTIVES' RELIEF SOCIETY—Maintains non-sectarian sanatorium for free
treatment and care of tuberculous patients.
JEWISH CONSUMPTIVE AND EX-PATIENTS' RELIEF ASSOCIATION—Treats tuberculous
patients who come to Southern California; non-sectarian.
JEWISH PUBLICATION SOCIETY—Promotes, prints and circulates books of general interest
on Jewish subjects.
JEWISH TEACHERS' SEMINARY—Trains men and women for Jewish teaching, research and
community service.
JEWISH THEOLOGICAL SEMINARY—Prepares men for the conservative rabbinate and teach¬
ers for religious schools.
JOINT DEFENSE APPEAL OF AMERICAN JEWISH COMMITTEE AND ANTI - DEFAMA¬
TION LEAGUE—Raises funds for combating anti-Semitic activities.
LEO N. LEVI MEMORIAL HOSPITAL—Gives care and treatment to indigent persons suffer¬
ing from rheumaitsm, sciatica and related ailments; non-sectarian.
NATIONAL FARM SCHOOL—Prepares city boys for careers as practicing farmers.
NATIONAL JEWISH HOSPITAL—Treats people suffering from pulmonary tuberculosis. Non¬
sectarian.
NATIONAL JEWISH WELFARE BOARD—Conducts special activities for Jewish men in the
armed forces; serves YMHA's and community centers.
RABBI ISAAC ELCHANAN THEOLOGICAL SEMINARY—Promotes study of Talmud; pre¬
pares students for orthodox rabbinate and trains teachers.

Jacksonville Jewish Community Council
1945 Beneficiaries (Continued)
YESHIVOTH—Six miscellaneous American yeshivoth, training students for the rabbinate along
traditional orthodox lines, derive funds from the Community Council campaign.
YIDDISH SCIENTIFIC INSTITUTE—Records, analyzes and publishes materials related to
Jewish life and trains students for Jewish research.
AMERICAN JEWISH CONFERENCE—Aims through joint consultation to arrive at decisions
affecting problems of rescue, post-war reconstruction and Palestine.
AMERICAN ASSOCIATION FOR JEWISH EDUCATION—Stimulates coordination of Jewish
educational facilities.
AMERICAN ACADEMY FOR JEWISH RESEARCH—-Fosters and promotes Jewish learning
and research.
DROPSIE COLLEGE—Offers graduate courses in higher Hebrew and cognate learning.
UNION OF AMERICAN HEBREW CONGREGATIONS AND HEBREW UNION COLLEGE—
Former stimulates Reform Judaism; latter trains for Reform pulpits.
Local and Regional Causes
ARMY-NAVY COMMITTEE (JWB)—Renders recreational, social and religious services to
Jewish men in uniform in the Jacksonville area.
FLORIDA STATE STUDENT UNION—Helps to make possible Bnai Brith Hillel activities on
Florida campuses.
HEBREW ORPHANS’ HOME—Located in Atlanta, this institution cares for dependent and
destitute children of the Southeastern area.
PUBLIC RELATIONS COMMITTEE—A specific activity of the Community Council, this com¬
mittee works toward effecting better relations between the local Jewish and non-Jewish
community.
TRANSIENT FUND—Gives financial assistance to religious itinerants passing through Jack¬
sonville.
BNAI BRITH YOUTH COMMISSION—Coordinates the activities of these Jacksonville groups:
Bnai Brith Girls, Bnai Brith Young Women, AZA and Bnai Brith Young Men.
JACKSONVILLE ROUND TABLE—Aims to create better inter-faith relations between Chris¬
tians and Jews.
Jacksonville Jewish Community
Council
During 1945, the Council continued its growth at
a phenomenal rate. The annual campaign raised
over $130,000, the highest amount in its history.
This represents a per capita giving that probably is
not excelled by any Jewish community in the
country. Workers in the 1945 campaign were
greatly heartened in their efforts by an announce¬
ment of profound importance. Under the will of the
late Mrs. B. S. Levy, probated in December 1944,
the Jacksonville Jewish Community Council was
named as one of seven beneficiaries of the Levy Me¬
morial Fund. In accordance with provisions of this
bequest, the Council will share equally with six other
organizations in the rents accruing from the build¬
ing occupied by Levy’s. The bequest is in perpetuity.
No restrictions are imposed in the will, other than
that the money is to be ueed by each of the agencies
for charitable purposes. The amount of the income
accruing to the Council will fluctuate with business
conditions; it will run into many thousands of dol¬
lars over a period of years. The first income will be
available during 1946.
The generosity of the late Mrs. Levy was doubly
important because it marked the first time in the his¬
tory of the Jewish community that anyone be¬
queathed a sizeable sum of money to any Jewish
agency. Furthermore, it constituted a recognition of
the Community Council as the responsible central
fund-raising and planning body of the Jacksonville
Jewish community. The effects of this bequest will
make themselves felt in the years to come, in im¬
proved local services, to which the proceeds of the
fund will be devoted. The bequest also may set a
precedent for other local donors to follow.
ASSUMING ADDED PROGRAMS
In describing the history of the Jewish Commu¬
nity Council usually the fund-raising has been
stressed to a degree which is not entirely justified.
The gathering of money for the various educational,
philanthropic and religious institutions should be but
one phase of organized Jewish life. It is a most im¬
portant phase, of course, for without it other com¬
munity activities could not be pursued. There are,
however, many other facets to community organi¬
zation.
Since 1943 the Council gradually has been taking
on more and more of the functions which a true com¬
munity council must assume. These embrace all
areas of Jewish life which are properly the concern
not of any particular group, but of the community
as a whole. Whatever gaps exist in general com¬
munity planning should fall within the scope of the
Council. Sometimes they may be filled through the

Jacksonville Jewish Center
CUB SCOUTS
Cub Pack 14 (pictured above) is sponsored by
the Jacksonville Jewish Center. It is enjoying a full
year of activities, under the leadership of Cub Mas¬
ter Nathan Friedlin, assisted by Sam Cherry and a
committee of which Robert J. Gordon is chairman.
Serving with them are Harry Katz, secretary of the
committee, and Den Dads Hyman Safer, Max Mir-
kis, Louis Goldberg and Perry Safer. Chief Den
Mother is Mrs. Nathan Dwoskin.
Included in Den No. 1, of which Mrs. Nathan
Dwoskin is Den Mother, are Cubs Melvin Friedlin,
Sam Gordon, Donald Lovitz, Benjamin Dwoskin,
Harvey Joel Selber and Stanley Ruby. Den Chief
Boy Scout is Morton Harris. Mrs. Harry Katz is
Den Mother of Den No. 2. Cubs are Paul Becker,
Stanley Kantor, Harry Katz, Jr., Matthew Robins,
Bernard Nachman, Sol Safer and Norman Spitzer.
Den Chief Boy Scout is Irving Bono. Den No. 3 has
Mrs. A. Rosenkrantz as Den Mother. Cubs are My¬
ron Blattner, Joel Bressler, Bernard Fraden, Norman
Greenbaum, Harold Horovitz, William Lassk, Morris
Mirkis and Stanley Rosenkrantz. Gerald Sohn is
Den Chief Boy Scout. Den No. 4 has Mrs. L. Gold¬
berg as Den Mother. Cubs are Gary Bloom, Larry
Goldberg, Neil Presser, Charles Mizrahi, Charles
Cherry and William Isaacs. Jerry Schemer is Den
Chief Boy Scout.
The Cub Pack already has enjoyed three out¬
ings this summer. On July 11 members attended a
picnic at Jacksonville Beach, during which a pro¬
gram of games, races and a scrap book contest were
the main items of attraction. Cub Master Friedlin
took color movies of the event, to be shown at a fu¬
ture meeting. A third outing for the Cubs was
staged during the month of August.

Business and Professional Women’s Division of Hadassah
Mrs. Felix Glickstein
Officers
President .... Mrs. Felix Glickstein
First Vice President Mrs. Maurice W. Goldstein
Second Vice President Miss Mollye Mizrahi
Recording Secretary . . Mrs. Herbert Panken
Corresponding Sec’y Mrs. Esther H. Rosenberg
Treasurer ... Miss Lottye Salzman
The Business and Professional Women’s Division of Hadassah is
that portion of the Senior Chapter which is unable to attend the regu¬
lar monthly meetings because of business and professional demands.
It holds its own monthly meetings, and closely follows the program es¬
tablished by the Senior Group. During the past year, the membership
of the Business and Professional group was nine per cent of the Senior
Chapter. Figures prove that the B and P Women fulfilled an average
of over nine percent on all quotas.
Besides its Zionist program, the B and P has contributed time and
energy to important local and national activities. Outstanding is its
participation in USO activities in the form of several Saturday night
dances, and in obtaining home hospitality for the service men and
women stationed in this locality.
The membership of the Business and Professional Women are to be
complimented on their participation in the work of Hadassah. In spite
of their lack of leisure time, they realize the importance of Hadassah’s
humanitarian scope, and they devote their time and energy to the es¬
tablishment of a National Home for the Jews.

OUTSTANDING EVENTS
September 20, 1944—Opening of Center Hebrew school
with large enrollment.
September 24, 1944—Registration for opening of Sunday
School.
October 7, 1944 — Junior Hadassah holds annual fall
dance.
October 11, 1944—Child’s Welfare Luncheon sponsored
by Sr. Hadassah.
October 17, 1944—Daughters of Israel hold annual Desert
Bridge.
October 24, 1944—Rabbi Morris D. Margolis resigns as
spiritual leader of the Center after 12 years of
service.
October 27, 1944—Dr. H. O. H. Levine, Executive Direc¬
tor of the Center, speaks on the topic, “On Coming
Events.”
November 1, 1944—Opening meeting of Daughters of
Israel addressed by Harry Gendzier and Harry Levine.
November 3, 1944—Chaplain Isaiah Rackovsky of Camp
Blanding guest speaker at Friday night services.
November 10, 1944—Young Judaean Sabbath adressed by
Lillian Schwartz, Vice President of National Junior
Hadassah.
November 19, 1944—Farewell and Testimonial reception
tendered Rabbi and Mrs. Margolis by the Center. All
groups affiliated wit hthe Center participated in pay¬
ing tribute to the Rabbi.
November 21, 1944—First meeting of parents and teach¬
ers of Hebrew School.
November 29, 1944—Daughters of Israel annual Thanks¬
giving Dance proves to be a very successful affair.
December 1, 1944—Rabbi David H. Panitz of Syracuse,
N. Y. occupies pulpit of Center.
December 13, 1944—Joint meeting of Sr., Jr., and B. &
P. W. Hadassah features report of convention held in
Cleveland and a movie, “Out of the Darkness.”
December 17, 1944—A. Z. A. and B’Nai B’Rith Girls
sponsor Chanukah Carnival.
December 31, 1944—Daughters of Israel New Year’s Eve
Dinner Dance.
January 5, 1945—Rabbi Maurice Solomon of Kansas City
occupies pulpit of Center.
January 8, 1945—Annual open meeting of Center mem¬
bership addressed by Rabbi Solomon. Center also
holds election of officers. Harry Gendzier re-elected
as President.
January 17, 1945—Harry Katz installed as president of
B’Nai B’Rith at meeting held in the Center.
January 19, 1945—Rabbi Uri Miller of New Orleans oc¬
cupies pulpit of Center.

sponsored by the Jacksonville Jewish Community
Council.
January 26, 1945—Rabbi Aaron Wise, of Schenectady,
N. Y. is guest speaker at Friday night services.
February 9, 1945—Chaplain Earl S. Stone, recently re¬
turned from overseas duty, addresses congregation at
Friday night services.
February 21, 1945—Boy Scout, Troop 14, Silver Anniver¬
sary, celebrated at Banquet held in the Center. Cub
Pack and Girl Scouts invited to participate in celebra¬
tion.
February 23, 1945—Boy Scouts conduct Friday night
services. Paul Canova guest speaker.
February 25, 1945—Six Young Judaean groups hold Pu-
rim Carnival. Proceeds go to Karen Hanoar Fund.
February 27, 1945—Center holds election of Executive
and Cemetery Committees.
February 28, 1945—Senior Hadassah holds successful
membership tea.
March 2, 1945—Jacksonville Zionist District conducts Fri¬
day night services. Mortimer May guest speaker.
March 6, 1945—Jacksonville Jewish Center holds Purim
Soodah. Dr. Max Arzt of the Jewish Theological
Seminary guest speaker. Cantor Samuel Vigoda
renders several cantorial selections.
March 9, 1945—A. Z. A. holds anniversary Sabbath which
is addressed by Beryl Weinstein and Melvin Margol.
March 9-10, 1945—Cantor Samuel Vigoda officiates at
Friday night and Saturday morning services.
March 11, 1945—Irving Davidson, noted humorist, speaks
at Center, sponsored by the Jacksonville Jewish Com¬
munity Council.
March 14, 1945—Concert given by Cantor Samuel Vigoda.
March 16, 1945—Chaplain Leo Stillpass of Naval Air
Station addresses congregation.
March 25, 1945—Children’s demonstration Seder held
Sunday morning.
March 25, 1945—Pierre Van Passen speaks at the Center,
sponsored by the local Zionist District.
March 28-29, 1945—Passover Sedorim held in the Center
with Student Rabbi Benjamin Sincoff officiating.
March 30, 1945—Rabbi Sincoff conducts Friday evening
services.
April 15, 1945—Roosevelt Memorial Services held, with
Rabbi Abraham I. Rosenberg of Congregation B’Nai
B’Rith Jacob of Savannah conducting the service and
giving the principal address.
April 17, 1945—Center Men’s Club organized at supper
meeting. Rabbi Sanders A. Tofield of Houston,
Texas, addresses gathering. Nat Shorstein elected
temporary chairman.
April 20, 1945—Rabbi Tofield addresses congregation at
services.
April 27. 1945—Young Judaean Sabbath conducted by
members of various clubs, with Rabbi Henry Wernick
of Suffolk, Va., as speaker.
April 30, 1945—The Farband and other Zionist groups
honor Rev. J. Robin with a banquet on the occasion
of his 70th birthday, with Rabbi Lehrman of Miami
Beach as guest speaker.
May 1, 1945—Rabbi David Panitz elected spiritual leader
of Center.
May 2, 1945—Daughters of Israel hold closing luncheon
to mark the end of a successful season.
May 2, 1945—Rabbi Arthur J. Lilywand, Exceutive Di¬
rector of the Unity Committee for Palestine, ad¬
dresses meeting of the Zionist District.
May 4, 1945—Daughters of Israel Sabbath with Chaplain
Sidney Greenberg of Orlando as guest speaker.
May 11, 1945—All youth groups affiliated with the Cen¬
ter participate in Mother’s Day services.
May 13, 1945—V-Day services held in the Center with
Chaplain Richard Singer of Camp Blanding conduct¬
ing the service.
May 20, 1945—Twenty-seven students of Sunday School
confirmed at beautiful ceremony with Rabbi Solo¬
mon Goldfarb officiating.
May 29, 1945—“Outlook for Minorities in Post-War
Europe,” topic of panel discussion at May meeting
of Jewish Men’s Club, featuring as speaker, A. H^
Hurwitz, Carl Alpert and Andre Bialolenki.
May 31, 1945—Closing exercises of Hebrew School.
June 3, 1945—Closing exercises of Sunday School. Prizes
presented to outstanding pupils of each class.
June 4, 1945—Opening of Center Day Camp with a reg¬
istration of 50 children.
June 9, 1945—Cantor H. Bettman conducts Sabbath serv¬
ices.
June 12, 1945—Cantor Bettman elected Cantor of Jack¬
sonville Jewish Center.

Some Facts About the Jewish Calendar
Though the daily life of the Jew is guided by the civil calendar, for religious purposes we
have our own calendar.
N
There are several reasons why the preservation of the Jewish calendar is of the utmost im¬
portance. First, it is an ancient legacy of the Jewish people and it links us to its past. The great
events of Jewish history are identified by their Hebrew dates. Second, the Jewish calendar is
essential for the correct determination of the Jewish festival dates. As long as there is Judaism,
which includes the observance of the Jewish festivals and ritual, so long will it be necessary to
preserve and perpetuate the Jewish calendar. Third, the Jewish calendar gives a distinctive char¬
acter to the Jewish people. Every people and every religion develops its own form by which it can
be identified and which gives it uniqueness. The Jewish calendar gives this identity and unique¬
ness to the Jewish people.
The calendar in common use throughout the western world is based on the sun. The Moham¬
medans, however, base their calendar upon the phases of the moon. The Jewish calendar is a com¬
promise between the two, and is based both upon the sun and upon the moon. The months are
reckoned according to the moon (12 months of 29*4 days each), and the year according to the sun.
In order to take up the extra eleven days (since the moon year has 354 and a sun year has 365
days), a whole month is added to the calendar in a leap year. Every second or third year there
is an extra month which is called “Ve-Adar” and Adar.
The Jewish calendar is a very old one and its history is very obscure. We do know, how¬
ever, that in ancient times the Jews determined the month, year, and festival entirely by the tes¬
timony offered that the moon had appeared and had been seen. The Sanhedrin was given the au¬
thority to hear this testimony and through it to establish the beginning of the month, the intercal¬
culation of the calendar, and the dates of the festivals. At first the New Moon was announced
through the lighting of fires on hilltops. Later, messengers were sent out to proclaim the date.
However, since it took time for these messengers of the Sanhedrin to announce the New Moon to
communities outside of Palestine, it was decreed that these communities were to observe the fes¬
tivals for two days instead of one. This added day was called “The second holiday of the Dia¬
spora.” An exception was made in the case of Yom Kippur, which, because of the hardship of
fasting could not be prolonged. Rosh Hashonah was observed for two days even in Palestine be¬
cause it was also the New Moon, the first day of Tishri. In still later times, the astronomical cal¬
culation of the calendar became so precise that the practice of hearing witnesses about the ap¬
pearance of the moon was discarded. Despite this, the second day of the festival in the Diaspora
is still observed by Orthodox and Conservative Jews.
Some of the Jewish months have 29 days and some have 30 days. The 30 day months have
two days Rosh Chodesh (beginning of the month), but the first day of the new month begins with
the second day of Rosh Chodesh.
In order to complete the adjustment of the lunar with the solar calendar the months of Hesh-
van and Kislev are irregular in length, sometimes having 29 and sometimes 30 days.
This adjustment also prevents the Day of Atonement from ever occurring either on a Friday
or on a Sunday, so that the preparation of food may not be disturbed because of the immediate
proximity of a Fast Day to the Sabbath.
The Jewish day begins at sundown and ends the following sundown. Shabbos therefore begins
at sundown Friday night, and Friday is called “Erev Shabbos,” the eve of Sabbath. Holidays begin
at sundown of the day before the first day of the holiday and the day called “Erev Yom Tov,” the
eve of the holiday. In order to be certain that the Sabbaths and holidays are properly observed,
it is essential that the candles be kindled at least 15 minutes before sundown.
The Jewish calendar appearing in the following pages is arranged according to the civil cal¬
endar. In the upper right hand corner of each month are the names of the Hebrew month corre¬
sponding to the English month in the upper left hand corner.

Jacksonville Jewish Center
BUILDING FUND DRIVE
GENERAL REQUIREMENTS FOR THE FUTURE
1. Synagogue. A synagogue capable of accom¬
modating 1,500 worshippers and decorated in such
manner as to make for reverence and beauty, more
so than do the present facilities of the Center. In
addition, a Daily Chapel, much larger than the pres¬
ent facility, so as to accommodate the Junior Con-
gregaton, as well as the daily minyon.
The present synagogue takes care of the seating
requirements of only a portion of the general mem¬
bership. At present, each member receives only two
seats regardless of the size of the family, which
negates the principle of family worship, thus setting
the child apart from the parent. The present syna¬
gogue is not decorated in the manner that would
promote the greatest reverence, nor is it conducive
to the evolution of a more decorous service.
2. Auditorium. An auditorium capable of seat¬
ing at least 750 persons and equipped to serve 600
persons at a banquet. Also to be equipped with ade¬
quate kitchen facilities, stage equipment, motion
picture equipment, cloak room accommodations, etc.
The present auditorium has shown itself on a
number of occasions to be inadequate for the pres¬
ent needs of the community, much less its utter in¬
adequacy for the future. All social affairs such as
dances, banquets, carnivals, etc., have been greatly
hampered by the present capacity of the auditorium.
Unless this is remedied with larger facilities, the
Center will be unable to cater to future social and
communal affairs, but will witness the removal of
those activities to other accommodations. It is an
accepted fact that the present stage, kitchen and
cloak room facilities of the Center are grossly inade¬
quate even for today’s needs.
3. Educational Facilities. A modern educational
building to care for at least twelve classes in session
at one time, equipped with modern facilities so as
to provide for the needs of a Sunday School of 300
to 400 pupils, a Hebrew School of 100 to 150; a
daily kindergarten of from 50 to 75 children; and
a Day Camp of 100 to 150 children, providing such
accommodations as proper offices for principal and
teachers, library, storerooms, etc.
Holding of classes in lobbies, the kitchen, audi¬
torium, ladies’ rest room, synagogue, and daily
chapel can not possibly contribute to the develop¬
ment of a good school, yet this is the present situa¬
tion. The figures given above as requirements are
minimum ones for the development that can be ex¬
pected in the next 25 years, which number of years
is the minimum that can be used in the planning of
future facilities. If Jewish education is to be suc¬
cessful, then the facilities for its development must
be extensive.
4. Recreational Facilities. To include a fully
equipped gymnasium, swimming pool, and all other
necessary facilities for a complete athletic program,
for lounging and organizational activities, and for
all types of recreational activities.
If our Jewish education proposes for youths and
adults is to succeed, there must be stressed the domi¬
nant characteristics of Judaism as a way of life. To
make Judaism attractive, then facilities for every
adult that can be incorporated into the individual’s
daily living should be provided by the ewish insti¬
tution. Especially is this true of the needs of the
youth, who should be able to find his every desire
and requirement within the confines of the syna¬
gogue, whether it be his Hebrew instruction, arts and
crafts, athletics, or cultural activities. The Center
Day Camp is an excellent example of this integra¬
tion of all activities into a Jewish way of life. This
entire reasoning is merely a restatement of the tra¬
ditional role of the synagogue as a place of worship,
study and of assembly.
5. Building Utilities. Such facilities as ample
storage space, executive offices, rest rooms, etc.
Anyone familiar with the present facilities of the
institution as to storage and office space is well
aware of this urgent requirement in any future
building developments. An institution must have
adequate office space for its staff. It must have suf¬
ficient storage space for its equipment and adequate
rest-room facilities for the use to which it is put.

JACKSONVILLE JEWISH CENTER
MEN’S CLUB
Filling a long felt gap in the Center organization,
the Jewish Center Men’s Club came into being on
April 17th at an organizational meeting held in the
Center auditorium. Rabbi Sanders A. Tofield of the
Congregation Adath Yeshurun of Houston, Texas,
the principal speaker for the occasion, outlined a
suggested program for such a group.
The following temporary officers were elected
to serve until the election of permanent officers to
be held in the early fall: Nat Shorstein, President;
Julius Levin, Maurice Haimowitz, Max Backer, and
Herbert Sandler, Vice-Presidents; Roland Krantz,
Secretary, and Joe Krestul, Treasurer. A Constitu¬
tion was adopted at the June meeting, membership
dues collected and a suggested program of activity
decided upon.
THE PROPOSED PROGRAM
As an Auxiliary. To furnish the manpower for
many of the Center projects, such as membership
contacts, religious activities, education projects, etc.
As a Reflector of the Will of the General Mem¬
bership. In this organization could be found the
means for an expression of the sentiment of the gen¬
eral membership of the Center as to the administra¬
tion of the entire Center program. Through this
medium, those responsible for the administration of
the Center could employ the Men’s Club as a “sound¬
ing board” of the reaction of the general member¬
ship, while at the same time, being able to inform
the membership as to the necessity and desirability
of action taken or about to be taken. In addition,
many phases of the work now undertaken by the
Board of Directors of the Center could be delegated
entirely to the Men’s Club.
Youth Work. To furnish leadership and direc¬
tion to the youth groups directly and indirectly spon¬
sored by the Center. The attention of every Jew
today is focused on youth work as the focal point of
all activities designed to perpetuate Judaism. The
system of youth groups now found at the Center is
as important for the proper education of Jewish
youth as is the more formal system of the Hebrew
School, Sunday School, Day Camp and Nursery-Kin¬
dergarten. The Men’s Club, in this field, has a tre¬
mendous opportunity to serve such groups as the
Cub Pack, the Boy Scouts, A. Z. A., Young Judean
groups, Girl Scouts, B’Nai B’Rith Girls, etc.
Religious. To become the leading factor in a
movement to improve the religious services of the
Center. The Men’s Club could become the forum for
the discussion of contemnlated improvements in the
religious services offered by the institution. It, too,
could furnish the manpower needed for rendering
proner ushering at all services, for developing choirs
and choral groups, and for the promotion of attend¬
ance for services.
Educational and Cultural. To be the leader in
the development of a program of adult Jewish edu¬
cation, so vitally needed at the present time. The
Men’s Club has an excellent opportunity to furnish
to the general membership general cultural activi¬
ties in the form of well planned programs for each
meeting. It could also assist and aid the overall Cen¬
ter educational program for children in various
ways, such as awarding of prizes and scholarships,
furnishing additional facilities, etc.
Good Fellowship. To develop a spirit of good
fellowship and comradeship among the members of
the institution so as to give to the membership a feel¬
ing of “belonging” rather than the status of paper
membership or mere charitable contribution to a
cause. The Men’s Club could also serve as a means
of extending hospitality to new members and to pro¬
spective new members, especially to newcomers to
the city.
Recreational. To offer certain recreational fa¬
cilities and opportunities available under the limited
facilities of the present institution, for the Center
membership. As a goal for the future, the Club
could become the sponsoring group for all athletic
and recreational activities to be offered by the Cen¬
ter, when its proposed building program encompass¬
ing complete athletic and recreational facilities, is
realized.

BNAI ’BRITH YOUTH ORGANIZATION
Sol Proctor
COMMITTEE
Chairman
Co-Chairman
Co-Chairman
Mrs. Edith Galoob
Mrs. David Cowan
Mrs. Isadore Silverberg
Mrs. Sidney Brown
Miss Cecelia Springer
Miss Bernice Margol
Beryl Weinstein
Sol Proctor
Harry I. Jaffee
Ozeas Marcus
Mrs. M. J. Reiser
Roland Krantz
Clarence Gherlerter
Melvin Margol
Sam Bergman
Sam Greenberg
Harry Katz
David Davis
Cecelia Springer
After several months of preliminary organizational
efforts the B’nai B’rith Youth Organization of Jackson¬
ville has finally been set up as the overall group for all
B’nai B’rith activities.
About two years ago it was noticed that quite a few
young girls who were organized under various names
were consistently showing up at AZA conferences and
conventions. The Supreme Advisory Council in Wash¬
ington called a meeting to discuss this problem and the
outcome was that the B’nai B’rith Girls were organized
and charters for them were sent out to groups all over
the country. Their programs and plans were guided by
AZA directors. However, AZA and BBG did not take
care of girls from 18 to 25 or boys from 18 to 21. The
B’nai B’rith Young Women and B’nai B’rith Young men
therefore were organized. Instead of a Supreme Ad¬
visory Council a B’nai B’rith Youth Commission was
set up with BBYO councils all over the country to take
care of local needs.
With the help of Mr. Milton Cohen, assistant Director
from Washington, Jacksonville set up its BBYO, with
Harry Jaffe as chairman.
Today we have a very large group of AZA boys un¬
der the leadership of advisors Clarence Ghelerter, Ro¬
land Krantz, and Melvin Margol. Sol Proctor is presi¬
dent of AZA. This group, as everyone knows, has a
long list of outstanding and memorable achievements
to its credit. The boys have worked very hard as indi¬
viduals and a group to make this one of the topnotch
chapters in the country. Today we can well afford to
be proud of them, for Beryl Weinstein, an AZA mem¬
ber, was elected national secretary of the entire AZA
organization.
Recently the first anniversary of the Jacksonville
B’nai B’rith Girls was celebrated. These young girls will
rise to the height of their activities in the coming season.
Bernice Margol is president of BBG. The BBG is open
to any girl between the ages of 1 4 and 1 8.
The newest addition to our youth group family is
the B nai B rith Young Women which was formed in
February, 1945. Girls from 18 to 25 have shown their
willingness and desire to work together by turning out
for active membership. As a new organization, BBYW
has made an excellent record, especially in its war ser¬
vice work. With the coming year, these young women
hope to make their record surpass that of the other
members of BBYO. Cecelia Springer is president of
BBYW.

LETTERS FROM NOTABLES
; CONGRESS OF THE UNITED STATES
House of Representatives
Washington, D. C.
August 10, 1945
To My Jewish Friends:
I wish to extend to you my greetings and best
wishes at this time for a happy and prosperous
new year.
I also wish to confirm my support in establish¬
ing a national home for the Jewish people in Pal¬
estine. In my estimation, the Jewish people cer¬
tainly deserve to have a national homeland in Pal¬
estine, for the wonderful part they have played in
helping to bring this war to a successful conclusion.
Secondly, they need a national home to rehabilitate
their war weary, presecuted brethren in a land that
they can call their own, and certainly they have a
legitimate claim on this land and I hope that we
are successful in prevailing upon the British Gov¬
ernment to recognize this claim. You may rest as¬
sured that I will cooperate with you in this matter
to the fullest extent.
Emory Price,
Member of Congress,
Second Congressional
District of Florida
3934 Park St., Jacksonville, Fla.
OFFICE OF THE MAYOR
City of Jacksonville
Jacksonville, Fla.
August 6, 1945
To The Jewish Community of Jacksonville:
As your Mayor, it is a pleasure indeed to extend
to you my sincere greetings.
As Americans we have a heritage rich beyond
comparison—a heritage which, working together,
we shall continue to protect and foster. As citizens
of Jacksonville we have a great future ahead of us.
Your group has contributed much to our city’s
steady growth and advancement.
To all of you go my heartfelt wishes for con¬
tinued prosperity and happiness.
Cordially,
C. Frank Whitehead,
Mayor.

===== PAGE 63 =====
MESSAGE FOR ROSH HAS HON AH
September 8, 1945
Senator Claude Pepper, Florida
On the occasion of the 5, 706th New Year’s Day in
the recorded history of the Jewish people, I want to
extend greetings and to express my hopes for better
times to come, both for the Jews and for all people.
In the long history of the Hebrews, there have
been many periods of crisis, many tragedies, many
Unspeakable desecrations and desolations, and des¬
pite it all, a constant, ever-growing, ever-finer
spirit of solidarity among Jews and between Jews
and other progressive peoples of the world.
The enemies of the Jews are the enemies of man¬
kind, the enemies of civilization. On this New Year’s
day, we can look back over the past few years and
see another triumph of that solidarity in our world¬
shaking victory over the Nazi hordes.
How costly that victory has been we all know. But
it behooves those of other faiths to remember that
it was the Jews as a group who suffered most, who
bore the most woeful burdens, who lost most, in
achieving that victory
Yet the battle was fought by the United Nations -
the United Peoples - of the democratic world. The
struggle was won by the sacrifices of all races and
all faiths on the cruel battlefields, whether military
or civilian, of civilization against savagery. In the
ghettoes of Poland and the slaughter camps of Nazi
Germany, as on the Normandy beaches and along
the Rhine, men and women who loved liberty better
than death accepted that death to win that libertv
for their kind.
Today, let us look forward, rather than back. Let
us, Christians, Jews, Mohammedans, Buddhists,
Hindus, and all other believers in the goodness of
man and the perfectability of this world, unite as
human beings with but one aim: peace and security
for the world and the individual. Then together we
shall go forward and achieve that aim, united in the
brotherhood of our universal good will.
I do not want to think of this Rosh Hashonah as
only introducing a new year. I want to think of it as
introducing a new world, a world where man shall
live with man in friendship regardless of race, or
color, or creed, throughout all the new years to come.
The task we all face, the greatest and noblest task
that has ever confronte^ mankind, is to make the
promise of this new world come true. May the year
5,706 see great strides made in the accomplishment
of that task!
Ahood’s Fruit and Sandwich Shop
219 Julia Street
Phone 5-9888

National Jewish Welfare Board
Army and Navy Committee
Officers
President Ira Stein
Vice President .... Mrs. Sam Weiss
Secretary .... Marvin Schulman
Treasurer ..... David Davis
IRA STEIN
Born of necessity, brought about by the existing national emergency, the Army and Navy
Committee of the Jewish Welfare Board was organized on February 6, 1941, and within a very
short time had arranged a program that would take care of the religious, recreational and so¬
cial neeas oí tne «lewisn men and women of the armed forces while on leave from camp.
Our committee, made up of representatives of all of the Jewish Organizations in Jack¬
sonville, has continued to serve the Jewish men and women of the armed forces by doing every¬
thing possible to provide them with the advantages and pleasures which they enjoyed in the
normal community iue which they left behind.
The religious part of our program has always been of the first importance. During the
High Holy Hays our ¿eiigious committee formulated careful plans for the reception of military
personnel. The first emphasis was on religious services, and secondly, on home hospitality.
During the r asso. er nondays our religious committee assisted in arranging for Congregational
Seders sponsored oy the Jewish Temple and the Jewish Center. A traditional Seder Service
was also hela at oamp Blanding for those men and women who were unable to leave camp.
This camp Seder was attended by 1,430 Jewish men and women and was conducted by Chap¬
lain iíicüaru onigui ci Camp Blanding.
Our Committee on Home Hospitality has continued to do a yeoman’s job in these difficult
times of rationing and lack of domestic help. The assistance of every Jewish family is urg¬
ently requested in carrying on this most important part of our program.
Our Social Affairs Committee, through the assistance of the constituent organizations of
the Jewish Welfare Hoard, continues to sponsor weekly dances and buffet suppers each Satur¬
day night and informal gatherings and luncheons on Sunday afternoons.
Through the extension program carried on at the YMHA the week end dormitory service
is of the utmost importance. Each week end approximately 75 Jewish servicemen are pro¬
vided with sleeping accommodations and morning breakfast. The YMHA also sponsors a Wed¬
nesday evening gathering each week, in addition to their other services and activities pro¬
vided for the men and women of the armed forces.

The Foundations Have Been Laid
By Pierre Van Paassen
Both in Palestine and in America the foundations
have been laid; in Palestine the physical framework
of the Hebraic Commonwealth, in America the ideo¬
logical patterns of a great movement through the
fact that hundreds of thousands of Jews are ready
to help translate into action the ideal of Jewish
national regeneration. Moreover, the war has shown
one thing, something on which the Gentiles were
never in doubt, but which some Jews sometimes fool¬
ishly deny — namely this: that the Jews are not
merely a religious community of great antiquity
(they are that, too), but that they are also a people,
a people with a territory of its own, with a history,
with wars, dynasties, a civilization of its own and a
great epic literature which has moulded the thought
of half the civilized world. The peoplehood of the
Jews is an undeniable, tangible fact for all the world
to see and to reckon with.
For that civilization of the Jewish people, Juda¬
ism is again ready and willing, trembling in the por¬
tals of time, as it were, to become a contributing
factor to the sum total of civilization by operating
from a spiritual-national Jewish center in Palestine.
By the radiation of its life-giving energy, it will re¬
vive the drooping and withering Jewries in the Dia¬
spora and make them, too, contributing factors to
the civilization of the peoples among whom they
dwell.
SPIRITUAL FRAMEWORK READY
The spiritual framework is ready and in Palestine
the physical framework, the economic, social and
material framework has been set up to receive, ab¬
sorb and integrate vast masses of Jews, all the Jews,
in fact, who are at present in distress in the Euro¬
pean chaos and who, as the two international con¬
ferences on refugees showed, have no other place
to go on earth but their own land, Eretz Israel.
What is the world going to do about it? Will the
world let Israel live, or must he again take up the
wanderer’s staff and recommence the doleful jour-
new to and fro in this Christian world of ours, driven
from pillar to post, leaving an imprint of blood wher¬
ever his footsteps pass? Or will the world at last
end the scandal of history and — in the new era
which stands before the door now that the war for
freedom “everywhere in the world” has been fought
and won—also give the Jewish people its rightful
place in the sun?
THE ISSUE MUST BE MET
This issue can no longer be evaded. The fate of
the Jewish people can no longer be referred to the
Greek Calends. If the Jewish people do not get
their commonwealth in Palestine now, after all the
magnificent contributions they have made to the
victory of democracy and after the immense suffer¬
ing the Jews have undergone, what chance will they
have in years to come, when everybody will have
forgotten—and will want to forget—what was done
by Jews in the years 1939-1945?
The establishment of a Jewish Commonwealth in
Palestine is by no means a lost cause. It need not,
in HerzPs words, be a fairy tale if we will it—that
is, all who love Zion and who understand what the
establishment of a Jewish State means to the Chris¬
tian world and to the Jewish people in redemption
and regeneration. A mighty, superhuman effort is
now required. That effort will be made in the next
few months. A fire is going to be lit that will sweep
away and consume all obstacles in its path.

Sol Zavon
In the twelve-month period just completed, the Jacksonville YMHA
has demonstrated that it is an important, even a vital, factor in Jewish
community life. Of course, the YMHA has been, above all, of service
to members of the armed services, co-operating with the USO and
JWB to the fullest extent. During the first six months of 1945 sleeping
accommodations were provided to 1,726 service men, Sunday break¬
fasts to 1,061, and Wednesday night open house affairs for service¬
men were attended by 1,037 appreciative persons.
The many facilities of the YMHA have been during the past years
of war, and will be until the need is ended, devoted to servicemen, yet
the organization has been surprisingly active in other important re¬
spects. Particularly noteworthy was the bringing, under YMHA aus¬
pices, of Sammy Kaye and his “Swing and Sway” orchestra to Jack¬
sonville. The dance, held at the Armory on March 15, was, according
to best informed authorities, the most successful and best attended af¬
fair of its kind ever held in Jacksonville. More than $2,000 was netted,
the profits being held in escrow for future development of the YMHA.
Plans for bringing a Broadway production of a smash hit to Jackson¬
ville in November, and other attractions of high calibre, are already
in progress, part of a program devised for post-war expansion.
In June, 1945, a landmark—the old YMHA building—was sold, the
money also being held in escrow to be used only for YMHA building
and expansion needs as incorporated in the charter of the original or¬
ganization. Meanwhile, the present quarters of the YMHA have been
entirely renovated and redecorated, presenting an attractive environ¬
ment to which the Jacksonville Community is always welcome.
On February 15th, the new officers of the YMHA were honored by
a dinner dance at the Roosevelt Hotel, attended by several hundred
persons. YMHA officers, directors and members comprising almost
two hundred men, are pledged to a continuation of service to the com¬
munity now and in the future.

Jacksonille Zionist District
ANDRE BIALOLENKI
Officers
President
First Vice President .
Second Vice President
Secretary-
Treasurer
Advisor ....
Andre S. Bialolenki
Herbert Sandler
. Joseph Strauss
Harry Sobel
William Silverman
Harrv Katz
It is a common axiom that we learn to do by doing.
By the same logic we learn to live as Zionists by
thinking and acting as Zionists. Zionism in Jack¬
sonville is and has been a growing thing, and like
all growing things, it has learned slowly. But this
is a mature age, and the time has come for it to
assume the responsibilities of its maturity. In the
past year, strides were made, strides that were
meant to bring inspiration to itself, and to send forth
inspiration to others. Three Zionist officers attend¬
ed a three day Regional Convention in New Orleans,
Louisiana. On behalf of the organization which they
represented, and at their own cost, William Silver-
man, Harry Katz and Andre Bialolenki flew to New
Orleans to attend meetings, to listen to leaders, to
learn, that the cause might be perpetuated and en¬
hanced.
Pierre Van Paassen, world famous author and
lecturer on Zionist themes, was presented to Jack¬
sonville by the Jacksonville Zionist District at the
Jacksonville Jewish Center Sunday, March 25, 1945.
The reaction was spontaneous and complete, and
many were enlightened with the truth of the neces¬
sity of our people for a homeland in Palestine.
The District had not made the comparative gain
in membership it had made in its Zionist ranks in
former years. Still there was some gain, and the
total enrollment began to stretch towards 300. This
is still short of its objective, but the road is long and
narrow, difficult with many detours of indifference,
hnawareness and prejudice, even among our own,
for enlightenment comes slowly and only with labor.
Zionist efforts towards the abrogation of the
White Paper continued. Efforts toward the increase
of the Jewish National Fund went forward with
greater vigor. The Jacksonville Zionist District add¬
ed its voice to the universal bereavement of men of
good will on the death of our beloved President
Franklin Delano Roosevelt. And when President
Harry Truman stepped into this great office, the
Zionists offered their prayers that he, too, might be
guided with the humane insight and profound judg¬
ment of the man who had gone before him, that
he might lead this country to victory and peace,
and our people to refuge in the land of our fathers.
The youth work was continued in Young Judean
classes meant to instill a sense of belonging and re¬
sponsibility in our young people who attended these
classes. During the course of the year Rabbi Arthur
J. Lelyveld addressed the Jacksonville Zionist Dis¬
trict on the meaning of Zionism and the need for
Palestine. Technicolor films on Palestine were ex¬
hibited to the membership. Harry Katz has served
and will continue to serve as liaison officer between
the American-Christian Palestine Committee and the
District. The District presented “Pop” Leiberman
with a Certificate in the Golden Book for his con¬
tribution to the Zionist cause. The District partici¬
pated with other organizations in the community in
honoring Rev. Robbins for his untiring efforts on
behalf of his people.
