“Anti-Semitism Is Anti-Christian”
By the
Very Reverend Raimundo De Ovies
Dean, St. Philip’s Episcopal
Cathedral, Atlanta, Ga.
Easily recognized, Jews can be distinguished by
their physical appearance. They are short or tall,
fat or thin, curly or straight haired, “hook” or snub
nosed, dark or fair, and speak either with an ac¬
cent or with one. One Jew I know is a tall, well-
built, blue-eyed, typical German “Aryan”—in ap-
»
pearance; and I once knew a secretary, a young
Jewess, who was a petite blonde with a retrousse
nose, whose nickname was “Tex.” Of course, many
Jews do look like our popular conception of the
Jew; but so do some Irishmen look like Irish and
some Englishmen answer the stage delineation of
an Englishman. How many readers remember the
comedy, “So This Is London?”
“Jews are rich. They all make money,” we are
told; yet all the Jews in the United States probably
could not match the fortune of Mr. Henry Ford;
and in Georgia, it is obvious that the majority of
bankers or other financiers are not Jews. Some
Jews are wealthy and a few of them are very
wealthy; but when we read the roster of Americans
and who control oil, steel, railroads, chemical, tele¬
graph and telephone facilities, shipping, coal, plane¬
manufacturing and other essential industries, we
find very, very few top-ranking Jews. . . .
The Jews do not have the same religious be¬
liefs as orthodox Christians; but neither do the
Jehovah Witnesses or a dozen or more other Chris¬
tian groups; nor do orthodox religious bodies al¬
together agree among themselves. One of my friends
who became an Episcopal minister out in Texas,
learned his Christianity (which embraces all the
arguments for the divinity of Christ) from a rabbi,
the celebrated Henry Cohen of Cleveland.
I do not quite agree, theologically, with my
brethren who are Presbyterians or Baptists; but I
have no quarrel with them on that score, nor have
they with me. We hold each other in affectionate
esteem although each of us presents his own beliefs
to the best of his ability; so why quarrel with the
Jews over their belief, if it is sincere? God himself
established the Jewish religion, if my Bible means
anything at all.
I believe that the gospel is a more full and com¬
plete revelation of God’s purpose, but I cannot
prove it by Jew-baiting,” nor can anyone else.
Anti-Semitism is anti-Christian. Christians will not
be a party to it. We leave that to Hitler and his kind.
They All Helped Make America
By Augustus W. Bennet,
U. S. Congressman, 29th District,
New York
Most of the racial and religious animosities
which exist in our country would disappear if our
people had a better knowledge of American history.
Anti-Catholicism would not thrive on a diet of
pamphlets describing the laws guaranteeing free¬
dom of religion established in what is now Mary¬
land under the leadership of Lord Baltimore, a de¬
vout Catholic.
We would hear less about “Polacks” if Ameri¬
cans knew more of their debt to Thaddeus Kosciusz-
ko for his help to our Revolutionary armies.
Italians like Columbus and Cabot played a large
part in the opening up the New World, and another
Italian, Amerigo Vespucci, gave our hemisphere
his name.
•v
Every school boy has heard of Lafayette, but
how many give credit to a German, Baron Von Steu¬
ben, for making an army out of a rabble?
Scots like John Paul Jones, Irishmen like Gen¬
eral John Sullivan and, of course, people of English
descent like George Washington, all pitched in and
worked for the common cause. At a later period a
Swede, John Ericsson, and a part-Soaniard, Admiral
David Farragut. were to Drove the heroes of the
Union Navy in the Civil War.
Whenever permitted to do so, our Negroes have
cheerfullv borne their full share of the obligation of
citizenship both in war and in peace.
Least understood and appreciated of all, per¬
haps. is the role played by a Jew in keeping the
Continental Armv in the field. An occasional stu¬
dent knows of the heroic efforts of Robert Morris
to raise monev for that purpose — but who ever
heard of the eon a 11 v patriotic and effective work of
Havm Solomon? For a task which broke his health
and fortunes. Havm Solomon e*ot two vowiavds—
the knowledge that he had helped mi°*htilv to cre¬
ate a great new nation, and death in a pauper’s
grave.
Thev all wanted to help—white aud black, Jew
and Gentile, Protestant and Catholic, native and
foreign born. Thev all did heln. Their descendants
want to help todav. but too oHen thev find them¬
selves thwarted bv preiudice based on ignorance
of American historw Let us all pledge ourselves to
end that ignorance bv every possible me^nc? and to
bring into full membership all these peonies who
each have something of value to contribute!

Workmen’s Circle
BRANCH NO. 441
Officers
Financial Secretary S. Carasik
Treasurer M. Cohen
Hospitaler S. Goldblatt
Secretary of Loan Fund J. Young
Treasurer of Loan Fund H. Fradin
The Workmen’s Circle, with general offices in
New York City, is a fraternal order, consisting of
73,000 members, and 760 branches throughout the
United States and Canada. The organization has a
sanitarium in Liberty, New York, and 160 Yiddish
schools throughout the country. It has a special
fund for all members, it pays various benefits and
issues insurance policies to its members from $100
to $3,000. The assets of the organization are $8,-
500,000.
The local branch meets in the Center every first
and third Wednesday in the month. It has a loan
fund for its members. Loans are issued from $25
to $300.
Riverside Memorial Park Association is the
Workmen’s Circle own plot.

LETTERS FROM NOTABLES
ZIONIST ORGANIZATION OF AMERICA
1720-16th Street, N. W.
Washington, D. C.
August 2, 1945.
Mr. Harry Gendzier, President
Jacksonville Jewish Center,
Silver and West Third Streets,
Jacksonville, Florida
Dear Mr. Gendzier:
I am enclosing herewith a message from Dr.
Israel Goldstein in compliance with your request,
dated July 26. I trust that this will prove satisfac¬
tory for your needs.
Accept my kindest personal regards.
Sincerely yours,
Dr. Sidney Marks
The Jewish community of Jacksonville, in look¬
ing back over the past year, can well be proud of
the record of its achievements. It has contributed to
the intellectual, industrial and social life of the
community; has played an important role in the
war effort and has acquired an enviable record for
the hospitality it extended to the many Jewish
youths in the armed forces who have been stationed
in the city. I wish to extend to the Jewish community
on behalf of the entire membership of the Zionist
Organization of America and myself, best wishes
for the coming year and to express the sincere hope
that those of our sons and daughters who are serv¬
ing our country so valiantly be returned to us safely
and in good health.
As I travel through the mournful and devastated
wastes of Europe, it is obvious that upon the Jews
of America will fall a great share of the responsi¬
bility for the rehabilitation and resettlement of
those of our Jewish brethren who have been res¬
cued from the Nazi hordes. Some of these unfor¬
tunate victims may find it possible to return to their
homes. In many cases, return is not only impossible,
but fraught with dangers. Most of the survivors need
a home, a haven, and security. They need Palestine,
and Palestine needs and wants them. The task be¬
fore the Jewish people of America is clear: to aid
their European brethren in their great need, to re¬
habilitate them so that they can once more live in
peace and safety.
The new year brings with it new courage and
hope and new faith. May it also bring an upright,
honest peace and a renewed spirit so that the
peoples of the world can once again build the foun¬
dation of a better world.
State of Florida
EXECUTIVE DEPARTMENT
Tallahassee
August 2, 1945
Hon. Harry Gendzier, President
Jacksonville Jewish Center
Silver and West Third Sts.
Jacksonville, Florida
Dear Mr. Gendzier:
On behalf of your fellow citizens of Florida and
personally I want to offer hearty congratulations
on the patriotic and public spirited accomplishments
of the Jewish community during the past year. This
splendid record is a reflection of past accomplish¬
ments and a promise of greater and more fruitful
services in the future.
With every good wish,
Sincerely,
Millard F. Caldwell,
Governor.
Dr. Israel Goldstein.

Progressive Credit Union
Officers
President ..... Alfred Stein
Vice President ... I. Lipson
Treasurer ..... Lazar Klepper
The Progressive Credit Union, organized in 1940,
is a savings and loan association, one of 10,000
throughout the country. The organization’s total
net assets are $56,000. The Progressive Union of¬
fers short term loans at a low rate of interest. Al¬
though general business conditions temporarily have
lessened the demands made upon the organization,
there have been many times in the past when its
facilities have enabled small business men to wea¬
ther a crucial period. The Union is chartered by
the State of Florida and its affairs are under the
supervision of the State Banking Department. The
Progressive Credit Union is an offshoot of the Jew¬
ish Progressive Society, one of the pioneer Jackson-
vIÍÍe~Jewish organizations.

The New Cantor
Rev. Harry Bettman, formerly of Congregation
Beth Shalom, New York City, will be in charge of
all musical activities of the Center, in addition to
his regular duties of conducting services. Cantor
Bettman served for twelve years at Congregation
Emanuel in Providence, Rhode Island, after which
he served for three years with the Roumanian Syna¬
gogue in Chicago, Illinois. He also has engaged in
considerable radio and concert work in addition to
his regular duties.
A native of Roumania, he came here while still a
youth. His early study of music was under the guid-
ane of his father, also a Cantor. He also studied in
Paris, later continuing his training in New York City.
He is a student of the well known Cantor Jacob
Schwartz of the Conservative West End Synagogue,
of which Rabbi Israel Goldstein is the spiritual lead¬
er. Cantor Bettman is married and has one son, Cor¬
poral Louis Bettman, at present stationed in Seattle,
Washington.
TO THE GREATEST BUSINESS YEAR — 1945
IN THIS SEASON OF SPIRITUAL REBIRTH AND SOLEMN RE¬
FLECTION, LET US FORGET THE MOMENTARY SHORTCOMINGS
OF MANKIND AND LET OUR HEARTS AND MINDS DWELL IN¬
STEAD ON THE CENTURIES-OLD AND BEAUTIFUL VERITIES
EXEMPLIFIED BY THE YULETIDE THE INDESTRUCTIBILITY
OF HIGHER GOODNESS, THE CONTINUING ASPIRATIONS OF
MAN TOWARD A HIGHER, BETTER PLANE, THE CERTAINTY
THAT TODAY’S STORMS WILL PASS AND A NEW AND BETTER
WORLD WILL EMERGE FROM THE BITTER TRAVAIL
WALTER T. BUGBEE
JAX SUNDAY TRIBUNE

Jewish National Workers Alliance
Brandéis Branch No. 295
Officers
President
Vice President .
Recording Secretary
Financial Secretary
Treasurer ....
I. J. Edelstein
. Max Mirkis
Charles Kroser
. Harry Slott
Mark M. Sablow
The Brandéis Branch was organized in February,
1944, with a membership of 12, which has now
grown to 55. Its activities for the year began with
initiation of officers on February 1, at which time
a banquet at the Seminole was attended by 200
guests. Louis Segal, national secretary of the JNWA
(Farband) was principal speaker. In May the Far-
band honored Rev. Joseph Robin, on the occasion
of his 70th birthday for his years of activity in be¬
half of the Jewish National Fund. Over $2,000 was
raised for the JNF. The main speaker at the dinner
held in the Jewish Center was Rabbi Lehman of Mi¬
ami Beach. Over 200 guests were in attendance.
I. Edelstein

FRANKLIN DELANO ROOSEVELT
January 30, 1882 - April 12, 1945
HARRY S. TRUMAN
May 8, 1884 -
LIKE LINCOLN, Franklin Delano Roosevelt died
at the hour of triumph. We mourn with all the
freedom -loving people of the world the passing
of a man who, during his long term as President
of these United States, had as his guiding star the
freedom of all mankind. We rejoice in his suc¬
cessor. We feel profoundly grateful that another
great American, Harry S. Truman, is given us by
Providence to carry on the great task. He can not*
will not fail.

FOREWORD
It is with a great deal of pleasure and pride that we present our forty-
second annual Year Book, the largest and best, we believe, that we have
ever published.
We wish to thank our more than nine hundred advertisers for their
.support and wish to take this opportunity to wish each one of them a
healthy, happy and prosperous New Year. Without their support this beek
would not be possible.
There are numerous special features herein valuable for year-round in¬
formation and that will repay your constant study. The English-Hebrew
Calendar may be referred to on every occasion where a holiday or memorial
date is required. Our complete membership list, will be referred to time and
again. The Honor Roll listing our boys in the Services stands as a recorded
tribute that will be cherished by many. The outline of our complete activi¬
ties, and of our plans and aspirations for the future, deserve careful reading.
The complete statistics on births, Bar Mitzvahs, marriages, deaths, and the
resume of our year’s activities are worthy of especial mention.
May we call our memberships’ attention to the directory of advertisers.
Their addresses and telephone numbers are listed, and we feel certain you
will want to patronize them—mentioning the Year Book when you do so.
Our thanks to all of the organizations that have so graciously co-operated
with our editorial staff. And, in closing, thanks to all who have assisted in
this effort.
CENTER YEAR BOOK STAFF,
HYMAN SELBER, Chairman

Flag Waves
By Bernard Postal
The first to raise the American flag on pre-war Japanese territory
was Lt. Col. Melvin Krulewitch of New York City, who hoisted the Stars
and Stripes on Namur Island during the invasion of the Marshalls.
Colonel Krulewitch was also in the Marine Corps detachment whose
■ members raised the Stars and Stripes on Iwo Jima.
❖ ❖ * , -. : ,•/ - .. , , .
'' ' ' °S
; The first American flag planted in the city of Algiers at the start
of the North African invasion was carried by Corp. Hyman I. Shakin
of New York. ;
(v H1 Hi s
’ < T • ij * ; «•* . ;s .
The only American flag bearing Hebrew letters was presented to
Abraham Lincoln in 1861 by Abraham Kohn, a member of Chicago’s
Ramah B’nai B’rith Lodge, a Jewish merchant and civic leader, shortly
after Lincoln’s election to the Presidency. Handmade of silk, the flag
j had inscribed on it in black letters the third to the ninth verses of the
■ t first chapters of Joshua. Although Lincoln acknowledged receipt of
-i. the flag, its whereabouts are a mystery. However, it is mentioned in
Admiral George H. Preble’s standard work on the flag, “History of the
< Flag of the United States.” • r
H< Hí ^
Although there are many claimants to the distinction of being the
' father of Flag Day, the late Benjamin Altheimer, New York and St.
Louis philanthropist and a Ben B’rith, is credited with having inspired
^ the first observance in St. Louis on June 14, 1912. Through the efforts
of Senator Spencer, of Missouri, who had been interested in Flag Day
by Altheimer, President Wilson issued the first national Flag Day proc¬
lamation in 1916. s
< Forty-eight different flags have entered the history and evolution
: of the American flag. Among the foreign flags that have flown over
some part of what is now continental United States were those of the
Vikings, Spain, England, France, Holland, Belgium, Sweden, Russia
and Mexico.
❖ * *
Flag Day is observed on June 14th because it was on that day in
1777 that the Continental Congress adopted the present form of the
flag as the national emblem.
Hi * *
The first American flag raised on Saipan was carried by Marine
Corps Sgt. Morris Becker of Chicago, who was later killed in action
at Iwo Jima.
• ' H< Hi *
- • I
Eddie Cantor; I. J. Fox, New York merchant; Harry Kastenbaum,
of Rockford, Illinois, and the late Benjamin Altheimer, all members of
B’nai B’rith, are among the 40 men and women who have received the
rare honor of life membership in the Order of the Flag, the legion of
honor of the United States Flag Association.
Hi Hi ❖
The ritual book of B’nai B’rith contains a ritual for Flag Day as
well as a brief history of the American flag.

Ladies’ Hebrew Sheltering Aid Society
Mrs. Nathan Dwoskin

The Ladies Hebrew Sheltering Aid Society has
served the community in welfare work both among
local people and transients for more than two dec¬
ades. Its non-interest loan fund has enabled many
families to overcome financial difficulties. Each
Passover money is sent to orphan homes and food is
bought and prepared for inmates of the state prison.
Our organization has cooperated with the U.S.O.
in sponsoring dances and receptions for the men
and women in the armed forces, under the chair-
f
manship of Mrs. M. Michelson. . .
3
This has been a year of great progress for the
expansion of the Old Age Home division of our or¬
ganization. The approval and hearty cooperation of
a number of leading business men has been given
for the building fund campaign, on the basis of
their knowledge of the necessity of such a project.
Changes in our constitution have been made to en¬
large the board of directors and admit men mem¬
bers, in order to avail ourselves of their assistance.
In April our organization purchased a colonial home
on a spacious and picturesque river front estate,
and plans are now being made to prepare samé
for occupancy. . \ V

Senior Hadassah
to
Mrs. Maurice Cherry

Hadassah, the Women’s Zionist Organization of America, started in 1912 by Miss Henri¬
etta Szold, today functions in 500 communities, and has as its objectives and purposes: The
spreading of Jewish culture and Zionist ideals in America through Jewish education; the
maintenance of a health and medical system in Palestine; the support of the Rothschild-Ha-
dassah-University Hospital and medical school on Mt. Scopus; the support of Youth Aliyah
movement; and the redemption and development of land through the Jewish National Fund.
The Jacksonville Senior Hadassah Chapter sponsors a monthly education program, holds
an Oneg Shabbat gathering each month, and at each monthly meeting some special Hadassah
activity is presented by a committee chairman.
Through its Medical Organization Fund, Hadassah maintains a countrywide network of
public health and hospitalization, available to all the inhabitants of Palestine, regardless of
race or creed. After the outbreak of war in 1939, when transportation facilities from other
sources were not available, every type of drug required by the Hadassah Medical Center as
well as 100 other institutions which Hadassah services in Palestine, has been sent to the Holy
Land through the concerted efforts of the tens of thousands of Hadassah members in America.
The Child Welfare work includes the feeding of some 30,000 children daily through a
school luncheon system; a school hygiene program for an additional 75,000 children, 50 infant
welfare stations situated throughout the country, and an extensive vocational education and
recreation project.
As its share in the building of a homeland for the Jewish people, Hadassah is lending
aid to the Jewish National Fund by regularly assuming an annual budget. The invaluable
work of the Palestine Supplies Committee, which operates sewing circles, results not only in
the alleviation of the needs throughout the Jewish National Home, but also helps build and
maintain morale in these war days.
Through the Youth Aliyah movement, more than 11,000 refugee children have been saved
and rehabilitated, during the past 10 years. These young people have come from almost ev¬
ery country in Europe including Germany, Austria, Czechoslovakia, France, Belgium, Norway,
Bulgaria, Turkey, Rumania and Hungary.
In local war activities, many of the members of the chapter here participate in the various
phases of war work, Red Cross, blood donors, war bond drives, clothing collection for United
War Relief, and regularly entertain service men with dances and suppers.

The Jewish Quiz Box
By Rabbi S. J. Fox
Question: What is the origin and signifi¬
cance of the six-pointed Jewish Star called Mo-
gen David (i. e. Shield of David) ?
Answer: This hexagram, formed by a com¬
bination of two equilateral triangles, has long
been used as a symbol of Judaism in synagogues,
sacred objects, etc. This symbol is not men¬
tioned anywhere in the Bible or in Rabbinic lit¬
erature. The earliest trace of it was found on a
Jewish tombstone at Tarentum in Southern Italy
which is dated as the third century of the Com¬
mon Era. It is later found on coins and in books
of the 11th and 12th centuries. It was adopted
by the American Jewish Publication Society in
1873 and the Zionist Congress of Basel in 1897.
There are to be noted various opinions as to its
significance.
From some sources, the six points appear to
be the indication of God's presence within and
about each of the six extremities of the living
world (i. e. North, West, East, South, the Hea¬
ven above and the Earth below). There are
many such symbols of the omnipresence of
Deity such as the shaking of the Lulab in six di¬
rections. It is called the “Shield of David" be¬
cause it represents faithful Jewish optimism in
the coming of the Messiah (King David) who
is declared to have six attributes. Others re¬
late the six points on the star to the six measures
of barley that Boaz gave to Ruth (Ruth III, 15),
which the Talmud explains as the symbol of
the six great leaders in Israel that were to stem
from her, among them King David.
Question: What does the blue and white
Jewish flag represent?
Answer: Even though the use of flags are
of ancient origin, this particular type of banner,
i. e. the blue and white stripes with the Jewish
Star in the middle of it, seems to have its ori¬
gin shrouded in mystery. Most sources attribute
its first official assignment as the Jewish flag
to the first Zionist Congress in Basel, Switzer¬
land in 1897. There are some, however, that
date back to the days of the Hasmonoim as the
flag of the Maccabees. The color blue was orig¬
inally the color of the banner of the tribe of
Judah represented by a blue emerald set in the
breastplate worn by the High Priest in Biblical
times. The color blue also represents the sea,
the sky and heaven above and according to R.
Meir, resembles the “Chair of Glory" (i. e. the
throne of Deity—Menachoth 43b). White has
always symbolized purity and perfection. Ei¬
ther the symbol of Judah, the only known re¬
maining tribe, or the sign of heaven, set off on
the background of the “white" of purity surely
is a deserving representation of the ideal of
Judaism.
(Copyright, 1945, Jewish Telegraphic
Agency, Inc.)

The Jewish “Best-Seller”
By Rabbi Abraham J. Bick
The siddur is, to use a colloquialism, the “best¬
selling book” among Jews. Billions of sidurim have
been purchased since the first siddur was published
some one thousand years ago. The siddur has sold
better, and is more popular, than even the Bible and
the Talmud. It has enjoyed popular success, and
has absorbed the interest of great Jewish theologi¬
ans, many of whom have edited or authored prayer
books.
Compiler of the first siddur, published in 1075,
was Rav. Amron Gaon, one of the greatest of the
Babylonian Jewish scholars. He compiled the siddur
at the request of Rav Yitzchak, son of Rav Shimon,
of Spain who paid him 10 zehuvim (gold pieces)
for the labor. Rabbi Amram’s siddur was the model
for all subsequent siddurim.
Rabbi Amram did not author the siddur, he mere¬
ly assembled prayers and grace that had already
been current among Jews for some one1 thousand
years, and, some, perhaps for two thousand years.
What did the early prayers consist of? The
psalms are among the chief sources of the siddur.
The siddur we now use opens with a poem by a non-
Jewish poet, Bilam, who came to curse the Jews,
and blessed them. “Mah Tovu,” he said—“how
good and beautiful thy tents, Jacob, thy residences
Israel.”
The siddur is one of the richest anthologies, in¬
cluding the most beautiful passages from the Bible
and the prophets, passages from Talmud and from
the rich garnery of Hebrew poetry over a period of
three thousand years, including poems by Rabbi Ye-
hudah Halevi and Rabbi Solomon ibn Gabirol. The
siddur also includes citations from the Shulchan Ar-
uch, the Jewish religious Code.
SECOND PRAYERBOOK
The second prayerbook to appear was compiled
by Rabbi Saadyah Gaon, one of Jewry’s major theo¬
logians and phliosophers, born in Egypt eighteen
years after the death of Rabbi Amram. His was a
“modern” siddur, including Arabic passages and
prayers he himself had composed. The Jewish peo¬
ple and its leading authorities accepted Rabbi Am¬
ram’s siddur as the standard prayerbook.
Although Rabbi Amram’s siddur was amended in
later generations, nothing was deducted, but addi¬
tions were made. Eventually two standard siddurim
emerged, with Rabbi Amram’s as the basis. One was
the Sephardic siddur, the prayerbook of the Orien¬
tal communities, and of the Jews of Spain and Por¬
tugal. The other siddur, Ashkenazic version, was
used in Germany, France and Poland. The Chas-
sidic movement in Eastern Europe adopted the Seph¬
ardic prayerbook.
Toward the end of the medieval epoch, and at the
beginning of the Renaissance, new prayerbooks ap¬
peared. Rabbi Isaac Lurie, father of the Kabbalah,
compiled a siddur jointly with Rabbi Chayim Vital,
his pupil. Another great Kabbalist, Rabbi Isayah
Halevi Hurwitz, also compiled a siddur. None of
these omitted or subtracted from Rabbi Amram’s
prayerbook. But they did add.
The eighteenth century saw the emergence of sev¬
eral new prayerbooks. Rabbi Jacob Emdin, of Ham¬
burg, trenchant opponent of the Sabbatai Zvi move¬
ment, the Gaon of Vilna and Rabbi Schneur Zalman
of Liadi, father of the Lubavich dynasty, each com¬
piled a siddur.
STORM OVER RABBI EMDIN’S SIDDUR
Rabbi Emdin’s siddur included an admonition to
the money-lenders of Hamburg. He described their
professions as “shameful.” The money-lenders raged
and requested that he omit the admonition. A com¬
mission of rabbis, appointed to investigate the sid¬
dur, directed that they had found nothing in Rabbi
Emdin’s prayerbook that might be construed as con¬
tradictory to Jewish religious doctrine. The money¬
lenders, however, unreconciled, boycotted Rabbi Em¬
din’s prayerbook.
Rabbi Schneur Zalman’s siddur met a similar fate.
He omitted certain prayers, and this aroused the
wrath of Rabbi Baruch of Mezshbizsh, grandson of
the founder of Chassidism, Rabbi Yisroel Baal Shena
Tov.
THE REFORM PRAYERBOOKS
There was quite a controversy over the first Re¬
form prayerbooks which were published in Germany
and Hungary in the first half of the nineteenth cen¬
tury. These prayerbooks, authored by the Reform
leaders Jacobson and Friedlander, omitted the pray¬
ers mentioning Zion and Jerusalem. When asked to
pass judgment on these early Reform prayerbooks,
the famous Rabbi of Presburg (Bratislava), Rabbi
Moshe Sofer, declared: “It is preferable to use the
Catholic prayerbook, than the Reform prayerbook.”
There are various Reform prayerbooks, including
those compiled by David Friedlander of Berlin,
Abraham Geiger, Isaac Meyer Wise, founder of the
Reform movement in America, and Rabbi Benjamin
Szold, father of the late Henrietta Szold.

Jacksonville Jewish Community Council
{Continued)
Council’s calling upon existing agencies to render
certain services not performed previously. Where
this is not possible, the alternative is for the Council
itself to undertake badly needed functions which no
other agency is in a position to carry out.
In line with this approach to community organi¬
zation, the Council’s new constitution, which has
been adopted by the majority of the Jewish groups
in the city, broadens its scope. It enables the Coun¬
cil to assume new responsibilities and implement
new activities of a community-wide character. Space
will not permit a full description of the new projects
undertaken during the past year. Mention therefore
will be made here only of three new developments.
ON THE AIR WAVES
A vital part of the Council’s efforts is supervised
by the public relations committee. In one area of
public relations, that of radio, the Jacksonville Jew¬
ish Community Council has developed something
unique. This is the weekly Commentator program,
now presented on Sunday at 12:15 p. m. over Station
WJAX. It has been described in detail in a leaflet
issued by the Council, entitled “Beaming the Mes¬
sage.” For that reason only a very brief summary
of these experiences is given here.
The public relations committee almost a year ago
felt the need of a weekly radio program, geared to
a non-Jewish public, to convey, through the medium
of news and a question box, information about Jew¬
ish happenings, personalities, customs and practices.
After a period of experimentation, a program was
developed which now compresses 2,500 words into
a weekly script of 15 minutes in length. After it had
been on the air for sometime, a poll of the listening
audience disclosed that better than one of every
three listeners of all four stations combined had his
radio tuned to the Jewish Community Council pro¬
gram.
The success of this program, the first of its type
presented anywhere in the country, has brought
many letters of commendation. Numerous local lis¬
teners submit questions to the Commentator, to be
answered during the question period. In over a score
of cities, community workers have indicated that
they, too, feel the need for a similar type of pro¬
gram. In discussing the merit of this approach to
Jewish public relations, one head of a national or¬
ganization commented: “It is valuable because we
need to multiply the devices that will trap and de¬
stroy false rumors and erroneous conceptions about
the Jew and his activities in American life.”
POPULATION AND WAR RECORDS STUDY
Throughout the years, no one ever has undertaken
even an elementary study of the Jewish population
in Jacksonville. Valuable data, in the form of let¬
ters, newspaper clippings and all the other items
that constitute the basis for community archives
largely have been lost to the Jewish historian of the
future. Even estimates of the size of the Jewish
group in Jacksonville have varied greatly. In 1937,
according to the American Jewish Yearbook, a local
estimate placed the number of Jews in Jacksonville
at 3,000. Yet, according to the 1944-45 edition of
the same book, the population is recorded as having
been 4,800 in 1937.
In the conviction that only a complete picture of
the Jewish group will provide a basis for intelligent
community planning, the Council initiated the first
Jewish population study, in May, 1945. Incidental
to the study, which is still in progress (June, 1945),
is the gathering of information for compiling the
war records of the Jacksonville Jewish men in serv¬
ice. This is important in order that there may be a
permanent record of their achievements, as a basis
for demonstrating the Jewish role in the war effort.
Beyond that is a more positive value—using the data
for future planning and the absorption of these men
back into the community.
SEMINAR
Jews cannot be intelligent about anti-Semitism
unless they themselves have an adequate under¬
standing of it. It is amazing how little some Jewish
people know of the elementary facts concerning
their own group. During the fall of 1944, as part
of its public relations program, the Council arranged
a six weeks’ seminar on the general theme “Anti-
Semitism: Its Causes and Cure.” A group of about
30 key people were invited to participate. The
course dealt with Jewish contributions to civiliza¬
tion, types of anti-Semitism, reasons for anti-Semi¬
tism and methods of tackling the problem. It was
hoped that this would be the means of indoctrinat¬
ing a number of people in problems of public rela¬
tions which daily confront the Jewish people.
The seminar was a success and evoked a great
deal of interest. The limited enrollment made pos¬
sible a full and free discussion, so that each person
could contribute his point of view to the sum total
of the group’s thinking. Originally it had been
planned to repeat the seminar several times, with
different people participating. However, these
plans did not mature. Campaign time rolled around
and no professional personnel was available to give
attention to the project. It is planned to continue
the seminar during the coming year, if the Council
can secure additional assistance, which is not now
available.

DAUGHTERS OF ISRAEL
l
OFFICERS
President Mrs. Charles Fink
First Vice-President Mrs. Jack Goldberg
Second Vice-President Mrs. Jack Becker
Third Vice-President Mrs. Philip Selber
Treasurer Mrs. Jacob Bloom
Recording Secretary Mrs. Haskell Zimmerman
Corresponding Secretary Mrs. William Gordon
Financial Secretary Mrs. Joseph Krestul
Asst. Financial Secretary Mrs. Isadore Gartner
The Daughters of Israel has served as the auxiliary
organization to the Jewish Center for the past 23 years.
Primary among the numerous communal activities this
organization supports is the Center Sunday School.
As in the past, it continues to award children for ex¬
ceptional achievement and to arrange the details of the
observance of the Jewish holidays.
One of the most worthwhile of its educational proj¬
ects this past year has been the establishment of a
circulating library in the Center Building. The library
was started by donations of the members, commemo¬
rating special occasions. In addition to a complete set
of the Jewish Encyclopedia, books have been acquired
on child and adult levels, dealing with Jewish religion,
philosophy, politics, and folk lore. This worthy project
encourages the attainment of knowledge and deep re¬
spect for the Jewish religion.
The Daughters of Israel is continuing its sponsorship
of the Girl Scout Troop and its interest in the Boy
Scouts. It has been rewarded for its efforts by the suc¬
cess of those organizations.
Mrs. Charles Fink
The members have been hostesses to several visiting
rabbis who have graced the pulpit this past year.
The Daughters of Israel have continued their active
and successful work in civic activities. The Red Cross
sewing group under the direction of its chairman, Mrs.
Charles Fink, met and over-subscribed its quotas; the
Red Cross bandage rolling group did outstanding work;
USO work was successfully carried on throughout the
year, under the supervision of Mrs. Haskell Zimmer¬
man; TB Seals were sold. The last project the Daugh¬
ters of Israel participated in was the National Clothing
Salvage Drive, at which time members sorted and packed
cases of clothing. They also take care of the upkeep of
the kitchen.

Jacksonville Jewish Center
NURSERY—KINDERGARTEN
This branch of the Center system offers one of
the greatest opportunities to lay the foundation for
intensive Jewish education. Here the child spends
half of the day in an environment permeated with
Judaism, at the same time being prepared for en¬
trance to the public school. The daily kindergarten
had an average enrollment of thirty children, al¬
though at times the number ran over 40. The school
was supervised by Mrs. M. J.'Reiser, assisted by Miss
Gertrude Meyerhoff. This school trains the child
in the observance of Jewish customs and ceremonies,
teaches simple Hebrew words and utilizes handicraft
for the creation of Jewish religious objects.

All Bnai Brith Committees have been very active this year. A Bnai
Brith Youth Organization was created, headed by Harry Jaffee, Chair¬
man. Under this Organization are three Youth groups, A.Z.A., B.B.G.,' '
and B.B.Y.W. Two B.B.G. delegates, Marcia Edwards and Bernice
Margol, attended a conference held in Washington on June 24th.
Our War Service Committee is likewise very active under the lead¬
ership of Sidney Brown, past President of Bnai Brith. Like the Youth
Committee, it is composed of an equal number of men and women. Sev¬
eral day rooms have been completed at the near-by camps. Regular
visits to the camps are made in the furtherance of our work. We spon¬
sor dances and other weekly affairs for the men in service. During the
holidays a large committee was in attendance at Camp Blanding and
rendered valuable service. We have placed several organs and game
kits on boats which were launched at local ports.
Under the able direction of Morris Witten, our interest and activi¬
ties in Hillel are always kept alive.

Message of Rabbi Panitz
THE AGE OF PEACE
Every age in history is named after its predominant
characteristic. The last several hundred years have seen
a r^pid succession of such terms as the Age of Reason,
the Steam Era, the Epoch of Science and other “Ages/
Today, the dawn of the Atomic Era raises its earliest
rays upon the horizon. With sheer breathlessness, we
wonder what advances lie ahead.
While none may deign to prophecy, it cannot be re¬
miss to suggest that the approaching period in man-
RABBI DAVID H. PANITZ
kind’s tortuous course shall be styled the Age of Peace.
Fully conscious of the consequences of our previous
sins of omission and commission, we earnestly proclaim
to ourselves and to our children and to the generations
yet unborn that nations shall not learn war anymore.
Not alone the sacrifices which have been endured, but
the bright possibilities which lie in store for Man through
global and local harmony and cooperation, impel us to
utilize our scientific and spiritual resources for peace.
So disciplined must we become in the arts of peaceful
living that historians will, without question, speak of the
middle of the twentieth century as the start of the un¬
broken Age of Peace.
The task of learning the arts of peace is far from
simple. A veritable structure of “Beliefs and Practices*’
must be developed. The unity of the Universe and the
equality of all men before their Maker must be com¬
prehended in all its practical, daily implications. All
peoples must be trained to enjoy the fruits of man¬
kind’s study and material progress. All persons, from
childhood, must be schooled in the love for democratic
living.
Religious institutions will be called upon to play an
important role in the education (for some, the re-educa¬
tion) of the nations. The great principles of religion
have been with us for many long centuries; unfortunately,
however, effective teaching for the application of these
ideals has been lacking. We must now devise success¬
ful methods. Precepts and principles must be translated
into life. Thus shall we truly herald the Age of Peace.
May this Rosh ha-Shanah be the harbinger of the
SHALOM for which we have waited so long. May the
year 5 706 see us girded with the courage which was
Herzl’s, when he said: Im tirtsu, ayn zo agadah! If you
will it, the dream shall become reality!

THE ESQUIRE CLUB
LIST OF OFFICERS
President Samuel Witten
Vice-President Eli Becker
Secretary Sheldon Gendzier
Treasurer Harry Yoffee
Sergeant-at-Arms.. Robert Bossen
Mascot.., Miss Vivian Rosenthal
The Esquire Club completed a successful season despite wartime limitations and restrictions.
Affairs given by the club provided clean, social recreation for many Jewish teen-age boys and girls.
At the first meeting of the year officers were elected. Samuel Witten succeeded Alvin Leit-
man as president. Miss Vivian Rosenthal was elected mascot. Early in the year a “smoker” was held,
at which time prospective pledges were introduced to the members. A hay ride was given followed
by other affairs at the homes of various members. At each a good time was had by all.
As always the climax of the season for the entire Jewish teen-age community was the annual
Esquire Spring Dance. This year it was held in the beautiful Cadet Room of the Hotel Windsor.
Music for the evening was furnished by a band under the direction of Bobby Walker, a senior at
Landon High School. Esquires, guests, and dates danced until twelve, when the curfew cut festivi¬
ties short.
During the year an Esquire Jr. Club was formed. It consists of boys of sub-Esquire age. It has
its own constitution and operates as an independent unit.
Esquire looks forward to another successful season in the coming year.
When in Miami be Sure and Visit-
Rosedale Delicatessen & Restaurant
IRVIN GORDON — HAROLD PONT
WISH YOU A HAPPY NEW YEAR
170 NW Fifth St.
Miami, Fla.

Jewish Welfare Society

The past year marked the twenty-sixth year of
service of the Jewish Welfare Society to the Jewish
community. Although the war has altered some¬
what the activities of social agencies, certain funda¬
mental problems still remain. Juvenile delinquency
has increased and countless problems arise because
of the enforced separation of families. In war as in
peace, there also are the aged and the ill, the un¬
employable and the family deprived of its bread¬
winner, all of which are the eternal problems of
the welfare organization. The Jewish Welfare So¬
ciety is a member of the Community War Chest.
David Davis

Bnai Brith Women of Jacksonville
Mrs. Maurice Galoob
Officers
President . . .. Mrs. Maurice Galoob
First Vice President Mrs. Harry Jaffee
Second Vice President . Mrs. Max Meyer
Corresponding Secretary Mrs. Sam Bryan
Recording Secretary . Mrs. Maurice Bartley
Treasurer .... Mrs. Sidney Brown
Auditor .... Mrs. Max Friedberg
The Bnai Brith Women of Jacksonville are
striving to keep up with every phase of war activity
as designated by the principles of Bnai Brith.
It has been a great satisfaction to us to find that
the name of Bnai Brith is known in our community.
Our local Red Cross Chapter has come to recognize
us as a significant group, one that can be depended
upon to work in any capacity. We respond to all
the Red Cross activities such as sewing groups, surg¬
ical dressings, knitting, blood donors, nutrition and
canteen aids, civilian defense work, salvage collec¬
tions, etc.
We are credited with furnishing eight day
rooms, servicing of four ships with portable organs
and game kits, two regular monthly visitations to
the Jacksonville Naval Air Station Hospital, U. S. O.
dances at our Center and Temple on Saturdays and
Sundays, and parties for service men in our day
rooms, in addition to affairs sponsored with our
youth groups.
We are geared to the war effort, in addition
to maintaining our normal activities, the further¬
ance of youth welfare, Americanism, defense of
Jewish rights, social and community service and
inter-faith understanding; all of which are inherent
in the American way.
Page Seventy-nine
===== PAGE 161 =====
JACKSONVILLE JEWISH CENTER
EDUCATIONAL PROGRAM
(Continued from page 47)
Grade Three: Prizes—Daniel Rosenberg, Irving
Cohen; Certificates—Leanne Balter, Rose Haber;
Attendance—Jay Shapiro.
Grade Four: Prizes—Bryna Lee Datz, Joan
Sachs; Attendance—Charles Mizrahi.
Grade Five: Prizes—Joe Abisch, Neal Presser;
Certificates—Irene Keller, Larry Goldberg.
Grade Six: Prizes—Stanley Rosenkranz, Freída
Hochman.
Grade Seven: Prize—Gilbert Geller; Certifi¬
cate—Marjorie Atlas.
Grade Eight: Prize—Beverly Safer; Certificate
—Hermina Doych.
Grade Nine: Prize—Nathan Krestul; Certificate
—Edwin Presser; Attendance—Fred Rotstein.
The Sunday School Staff consisted of the follow¬
ing: Philip Selber, Principal; Carl Alpert, Mrs. Carl
Alpert, Major J. Reiser, Mrs. Irving Rothstein, Miss
Eunice Davis, Mrs. Norman Moscovitz, Mrs. J. Sha¬
piro, Miss Gertrude Meyerhoff, Miss Esther Smith,
Miss Victoria Levy, Mrs. M. J. Reiser, Miss Mildred
Pinner and Miss Sylvia Haber.
CONCLUSION
Although the educational activities of the insti¬
tution were carried on during the past year without
the leadership of a Rabbi or of an educational direc¬
tor, it is a conceded fact that the educational pro¬
gram has witnessed its greatest progress in a num¬
ber of years. Much of the credit for this improve¬
ment can be attributed directly to the untiring and
sincere devotion of Mrs. Carl Alpert who acted, not
only as teacher, but also as principal of the Hebrew
School and as director of the Summer Day Camp.
Without the considerable expenditure of energy
made by Mrs. Alpert during the year, the success
of the Junior Congregation, the increase of the He¬
brew School enrollment, and the outstanding success
of the Day Camp in its first year would not have
occurred. However, the capable assistance rendered
by Mrs. Harry Abrams and Miss Gertrude Meyer¬
hoff were of considerable value to the success of the
program.
Rabbi David H. Panitz, who assumes the spir¬
itual leadership of the institution this fall, will su¬
pervise all of the educational activities of the insti¬
tution, and a very promising future seems to be in
the offing. Mrs. Panitz will also be of considerable
aid in the administration of the educational activi¬
ties, especially that of the Nursery Kindergarten,
since she is a graduate Hebrew teacher in her own
right.
THE HEBREW SCHOOL

I extend to all my fellow Americans of Jewish
faith my hearty congratulations and best wishes
for New Year's Day.
The enemies of civilization who would have de¬
stroyed completely all freedom of religion have been
defeated. All faiths unite in thanksgiving to Al¬
mighty God on our victory over the forces of evil.
Let us now all join to create the kind of peace
settlement which will keep alive freedom of religi¬
ous belief all over the world, and prevent the recur¬
rence of all this misery and destruction. That is the
most fitting memorial we can erect to those who
have fought and suffered and labored and died in
this struggle to preserve decency for mankind.

Rabbi David Panitz
A Biographical Sketch
Rabbi David H. Panitz was born in Baltimore,
Md., where he received his early schooling. At six¬
teen ,he entered the Johns Hopkins University and
continued his studies there until five vears later,
when the Master of Arts degree was conferred upon
him by the Faculty of Philosophy for his researches
in the Responsa of 15th century Italy. His residence
for the doctor’s degree having been completed by
this time, the Rabbi-to-be left Johns Hopkins for
the Theological Seminary. At the present time, he
is preparing his doctoral thesis for submission.
When Rabbi Panitz entered the Jewish Theologi¬
cal Seminary of America, he already had a sound
preparation in a variety of studies. He had been
graduated from the Baltimore Hebrew College and
was the recipient of the Teacher’s Certificate from
the Teacher’s Training School of the Hebrew Col¬
lege. He had gained first-hand knowledge of the
problems of Jewish education through experience
as a teacher and as a principal in the Hebrew schools
of Baltimore.
Following his ordination from the Seminary,
Rabbi Panitz was called to Syracuse, N. Y., to be¬
come the spiritual leader of the conservative Con¬
gregation Adath Yeshurun, the largest eastern con¬
gregation between New York and Cleveland. Adath
Yeshurun has been acclaimed for its superb and va¬
ried program, designed to meet the needs of every
age and interest. Among other activities, its Adult
Institute is the pride of Upper New York State, and
its bulletin has been honored as “the best in the
United States.” The religious schools, the clubs, the
Men’s Club, the Sisterhood and the Junior Congre¬
gations of Adath Yeshurun serve as the model for
many other congregations.
In Syracuse, Rabbi Panitz was active in many
Jewish and civic endeavors. At the Syracuse Uni¬
versity, he was a member of the Hendricks Chapel
staff and religions counsellor to the Jewish students
on the campus. He was co-chairman of the Syracuse
War Fund and Community Chest, Jewish represen¬
tative on the Syracuse Inter-Faith Committee, and
a member of the Executive Committee and chairman
of the Education Committee of the Syracuse Inter-
Racial Federation. In addition, he was the Mayor’s
appointee to the local Post-War Planning Council.
Among his Jewish affiliations, Rabbi Panitz was
a member of the Board and also of the Executive
of the Syracuse Jewish Welfare Federation, a Board
member of the Jewish Social Service Bureau, a mem¬
ber of the Syracuse Commission on Jewish Educa¬
tion, and served as president of the Louis Marshall
Lodge of Bnai Brith.
Ever since he spent some time in Palestine as a
child, Rabbi Panitz has been a devoted Zionist. He
was Program Chairman of the Syracuse Zionist Dis¬
trict, a member of the Executive Committee of the
United Palestine Appeal, and a member of the
American Zionist Emergency Council and chairman
of its church contacts in Syracuse. Rabbi Panitz was
the chairman of the New York Federation Drive of
the Jewish Theological Seminary. As a member of
the Rabbinical Assembly of America, he served on
several of its committees. Rabbi Panitz is recognized
as an important speaker and preacher. Having been
called to address many different gatherings, he was
chosen to give the Convention sermon at the 30th
Anniversary convention of the United Synagogue of
America.

YOUNG JUDAEA
Gerald Sohn
— •
Stanley Rosenkrantz
Stanley Kantor
Young Judaea began its year with four active
clubs participating in the Tag Day collection on
Balfour Day. The monies collected went to Jewish
National Fund. In the next month, two more clubs
were formed, making a total of six clubs, one sen¬
ior, two intermediate and three junior clubs. Two
intermediate clubs and three junior clubs held
Chanukah parties during the week of the Chanukah.
All the clubs were active in debating and round
table discussions, the intermediates debating for
a Senior Hadassah Oneg Shabbat. They chose as
their topic: “Resolved: That the Young Judaeans
Should Go to Palestine to Live.” Both sides were
eloquent, the winners being the Chaim Weizmann
Club, who chose the negative. Two Young Judaean
Sabbaths were held, one in October and the other
in May.
In February, the season was highlighted with a
Purim Carnival, all clubs participating. The spirit of
the approaching affair ran high, the community be¬
coming quite youth-conscious. The clubs built booths
at the Center in an area representing Tel Aviv. They
displayed and sold their handiwork in these booths,
a prize being awarded to the Aliyah Club for the
most attractive booth. The Meyer Levin Club pre¬
sented a puppet show appropriate for Purim. The
Henrietta Szold undertook as their project the sell¬
ing of food, which took place at the “Tel Aviv Cafe.”
Sideshows and races were added attractions. Our
affair was quite a success, both socially and finan¬
cially, monies going to Keren Hanoar and Youth
Aliyah quotas.
During the month of March, all clubs used the
Passover theme, several clubs making Passover plates
and holding appropriate meetings. The Henrietta
Szold Club held a modern Third Night Seder. In
May, the juniors clubs held a joint Mother’s Day tea
and program, at which all Young Judaean mothers
were honored. It was impressive and dignified.
All clubs have participated in hikes and outings,
centered around life in Palestine and Palestinian
colonies. The Senior Club participated in War Bond
Drives. In the latter part of June, Young Judaea
sent four delegates to the Southeastern regional
conference held in Memphis. These boys and girls
returned imbued with the spirit that prevailed and
in turn will inspire our members with the enthusiasm
for carrying on Zionist Youth work. Our delegates
bid for the next conference to be held in Jackson¬
ville, which was duly accepted by the region. We
are looking forward to next year’s activities and
conference in Jacksonville. Our delegates were
Martha Moscovitz and Mildred Edwards from the
Henrietta Szold Club, and Billy Schemer and Helen
Witten from the Meyer Levin Club.
The six clubs, their presidents and leaders this
past year were: Henrietta Szold Club, Doris Singer,
president; Mrs. I. Moscovitz, leader; Meyer Levin
Club, Billy Schemer, president, Mrs. M. Goldstein,
leader; Chaim Weizman Club, Gerald Sohn, presi¬
dent; Mrs. Sophie Ruby, leader; Aliyah Club, Stan¬
ley Rosenkranz, president, Mrs. M. Wasserman,
leader, and Miss Renee Baker, co-leader; Theodore
Herzl Club, Stanley Kantor, president, Miss Gert¬
rude Meyerhoff, leader; Louis D. Brandéis Club,
Sally Felson, president, Miss Heidy Mayer, leader.
Brandéis Campers Institute gave us two of our
outstanding leaders, Gertrude Meyerhoff and Heidy
Mayer. Next year others will return groomed for the
job of leading our young Zionists. They will also
be able to assist in the training of leaders who will
not have attended Brandéis Camp, by participating
at our regular leaders’ institute meetings.

The Reconstructionist Movement
By David Schwartz
There is a considerable fuss now about the Recon-
structionist movement. The present writer cannot
profess to know very much about Reconstructionism.
Perhaps the present stir—the advertisement given
Prof. Kaplan by his excommunication—may serve to
attract attention to the movement and lighten more
people about Reconstructionism who otherwise
would never have heard about the movement.
Those who condemn Rabbi Kaplan should have
considered this. As a result of his excommunica¬
tion, probably ten people have heard about Recon¬
structionism to the one who had heard about it be¬
fore.
As we say, we belong to the average. The pres¬
ent writer had only vaguely heard about Recon¬
structionism. Two years ago, attending a rabbinical
convention and hearing the term "Reconstruction-
ism, ” I asked a rabbi what it meant.
"Well, IT1 tell you a little incident," replied the
rabbi with a twinkle in his eye. "I was sitting in a
restaurant and close to me sat another rabbi—a dele¬
gate to the convention but a stranger to me. I saw
him eat some bacon. Ah, I said, he’s a Reform Jew.
Later, I saw him eating some herring. Ah, I said,
Now I know. He’s a Reconstructionist.’’
I suppose the Rabbi who told me this story was
only being humorous. And yet the story, as I gath¬
ered, does give you a slant at the Reconstructionist
movement, for Reconstructionism has taken from
both the Reform and the Orthodox wing. It has fea¬
tures of both of these groups. It is a new synthesis.
MAIMONIDES A RECONSTRUCTIONIST
Now, every new synthesis is not a desirable thing
and I hold no brief for Reconstructionism. "The mys¬
teries I leave to the Lord our God,’’ as our prayer-
book says, and to the theologians, but I recall that
Prof. Kaplan is not the first man in Jewish history
to indulge in Reconstructionism. In the twelfth cen¬
tury, there lived a great Jewish thinker, the greatest
Jew to concern himself with religious matters since
the days of the Talmud. The man’s name was Moses
ben Maimón. This man profoundly affected Jewish
theology. Indeed, his genius was so great that it
couldn’t help but spill over, and the Christian church
borrowed much from him.
Maimonides or Moses ben Maimón was also a re¬
constructionist in a way. In the first place, he
warned against the orthodoxy which straightway
condemned all deviators. He pointed out that even
in Biblical days idolators were still regarded as be¬
longing to the house of Israel. He had certain no¬
tions about miracles which some of the pious looked
at askance. He did not deny completely that there
were miracles. He held that at times a special Provi¬
dence might intervene. But, nevertheless, his com¬
ments on miracles did not escape condemnation
among some of the very religious.
Similarly, too, his ideas on the resurrection of the
dead were looked on suspiciously. Here, too, he did
not deny the dogma of resurrection, but the little
space he gave this doctrine and his emphasis on the
immortality of the soul was a kind of reconstruc¬
tionism. He was a reconstructionist with a small
"r” but still a reconstructionist.
Maimonides came at a time, the historian Graetz
tells us, when Judaism was faced by very difficult
times. In some places, there was danger of Judaism
disappearing. Maimonides proved a great fortify¬
ing and unifying force.
JUDAISM FACES SIMILAR CRISIS
I do not think it is any use denying that Judaism
today faces a similarly great crisis. The orthodox
rabbis cannot ostrichlike hide their heads in the
sands to save themselves from the storm ahead. You
cannot negate a thing by closing your eyes to it,
even though Reconstructionism, as exemplified by
the doctrines of Rabbi Kaplan, may not be the sav¬
ing force we need.
Maimonides tried to reconcile Judaism with the
philosophy of Aristotle. That was the reigning phil¬
osophy of the intellectual of his day. We need some
Maimonides to reconcile religion with the realities of
today.
Who will solve that problem for us?
One thing we know: burning books do not solve
problems. Some of the over-pious burnt the books of
Maimonides, but as one of his disciples wrote:
"Elijah like, they to heaven ascended
And in flames sky-ward wended.’’
(Jewish Telegraphic Agency)
Draper’s New Way Poultry Co.
